RATING: 9
Descent is D&D after it has dropped the pretense that it has anything to do with role playing. It is all about the hack and slash on a board with beautiful production value. What isn't to like about Descent? It is a great dungeon crawl simulater where one player takes on the role of the GM and 4 others take on the role of an adventuring party on a quest. The game comes with plenty enough quests for a campaign and there is a lot of support for it. The only real drawbacks are that it requires more of a long term committment than I can reasonably expect from my regular game night which is more geared to multiple games every night and different ones each week and that it can take a toll to always be one player who does a lot of set up and prep work versus four others who are required to be invested over a long period of time. I've been playing around with the idea of having a home descent night for a campaign some time...
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This game improves on the first edition so heavily that I don't think I would be able to accurately describe it. It's faster to play, there's more depth to the heroes, the minis are more detailed, the Overlord can win without taking any of the fun away from the heroes, there are more mechanics and more varied winning conditions, the tiles are double sided with outside  locations so you're effectively getting 48 dungeon tiles and 48 forest tiles, and the game isn't weighed down with all the unnecessary things the first one was (like the set-up time - the tiles are numbered and lettered, there are no tokens for holes and rocks, and there's an easier way to keep track of money rather than piling up the coins). It's far superior and utterly replaced the first edition for me, PHENOMENAL game!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.2
Descent is one of the best miniature games I have ever played, where a group of players can band up against the Overlord player which can control some cool creatures like dragons etc. The theme is great, the miniatures are AWESOME and the fact that you have different scenarios, different skills to choose from, different items, different characters, different Overlord cards etc. offers you a great deal of variety.
Your characters do not have a lot of health, and a decent hit could consume half of your hit points in in an instance. This creates tension, you never know you will live the next turn (well technically, you don't die but are just knocked down). However, this brings along that combat can feel really swingy at times, and also too luck-based. One turn you are winning without question, the other you feel like crying and giving up. Although I like games where there is some mechanism to deal with a runaway leader problem, I think they overdid it with this one. Another problem is the set-up, which can take a long time. The disadvantages do not outweigh the advantages, but I can imagine people put off be this. Still a wonderful game!

Theme (artwork/story/...)
2/2
Gameplay (smooth play/player interaction/strategy&tactics/depth)
4/5
Fun factor (can you afford a smile during play?)
2.5/3

RATING: 7
The base edition can be pretty boring for the Overlord as they have no possible way to win any encounter, even when using bizarre strategies. The game is NOT a dungeon crawler or even a tactical game. It's a racing game with asymmetrical play as every scenario is about completing objectives faster. That said, the combat system and items are really fun and the hero classes add a lot of replayability.

Update 2016: If you download the android app for this game and run THOSE campaigns, this easily gets a +1 to its score!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5.5
To me, this just felt like someone took a generic MMORPG and put it on a board. Coming from World of Warcraft (on PC), it's just not a comparison - and I didn't even care for that. It plays too slow, so you don't get a sense of panic or anything, and the decisions aren't very interesting, so there's not much for you to think about while playing, either.

For me, I'd rather play something like Space Hulk: Death Angel, The Lord of the Rings Card Game, Apocalypse Chaos, or TIME Stories. All of these (except TIME Stories) do a better job of evoking a sense of panic, as you play, and provide a real battle mechanism for the fights. Or, in the case of TIME Stories, it has a better system for telling a story, while the game is still just a "roll to see if you succeed" style game.

When playing an MMO, you have to work out a game plan in advance, coordinate in real time, prepare the right supplies, get your timing right, and so on. There's some amount of randomness in a big fight, but it can be completely mitigated by the proper tactics and preparation. Getting that all into place takes skill and effort to accomplish.

Or if you're playing a traditional tabletop RPG, really the hope is that the players will come up with clever ways to defeat the enemy beyond just "I use my sword", "Okay, roll 2 d10...good, you hit him for 2 DP." It's supposed to be centered on storytelling and improvisation. The dice serve purely as a mechanism for the DM to simulate any random idea under the sun that you might have.

All of that is lacking in Descent. It's not interesting from a storytelling nor mechanical sense. There's no game to the game. But all of that said, I suspect that this is largely true of most dungeon crawl board games and, probably, most people aren't looking for more than that. Descent seems to be the most popular title in this area, thanks to how streamlined the gameplay is. So, if you enjoy a game where you're rolling a dice and seeing what happens, then this is supposedly the best of the bunch.

Personally, I'll play those other games.

RATING: 8
A fun game of dice rolling, dungeon bashing, and monster slaying.
Descent has a well thought out combat system with a good mechanic involving spending possible 'surges' for additional combat abilities.
Another good addition involves heroes trading a limited pool of fatigue tokens for additional movement or abilities.
The overlord has control of all the monsters and a handfull of cards that do nothing to help the heroes whatsoever.

The game is fun when playing as either the heroes or the overlord, the only drawback is the price tag.

RATING: 7
I liked HeroQuest as a kid and this seemed like a more grown up version of that little nostalgia bomb.  Basically, it did exactly that.  I haven't been able to get it on the table with my regular group (for a full 4 player + 1 overlord game).  My only plays are with my wife (me as overlord, and she will play 2-3 heroes).  I had the rules screwed up for the first couple of tries and my wife (I had monsters taking 2 attack actions when they didn't move... whoops!).  She got understandably frustrated since basically, she couldn't win.  We tried again with the correct rules and it went a lot better. 

I think that the one sided beating she was getting when I wasn't playing the mosters correctly kind of soured her on the game, so it might be a while before it sees the table again.  

Overall, I like the game.  I'm not sure it will see more than one campaign with my group.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
After trading away the first edition I was pretty sure I didn't need this one in my life.  However I kept hearing so many good things about the new edition.  It sounded like this new version fixed a lot of the problems that I had with the old.

Turns out that it did.  Descent 2.0 plays faster and is more streamlined experience, with campaign rules built in.  Plus Fantasy Flight abandoned their enormous "coffin-boxes" (which were a pain to store) in favor of the more shelf-friendly square (also referred to as "ticket-to-ride style box").

I've played a couple times now and I like it...but I don't think I need to own it (especially now that one of the guys in my group has a copy).  It's fun enough but it just doesn't light my world on fire.  

I think what sets it apart from other, more interesting tactical skirmish games, is the terrain.  The maps are so small that there isn't many opportunities for maneuver.

Plus there are few spaces on the map that work differently than any other space.

This is not to say that there are no opportunities for clever moves (because there are), it all just feels a little like the game runs on rails.

It's a great production and a step up from the original edition.  I'm happy to play it - especially since the social experience DOES come close to replicating that RPG feel.  It won't make my list of all-time greats though.

*****

Update:  See my full review https://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/1418078/its-decent-review-after-full-campaign

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Descent is the go-to dungeon crawler for the present era. Though it's basically a tactical skirmish game, the adventure scenarios and campaign option provide a richer context. A Game Master (Overlord) is needed, but this role is more than a facilitator -- they are vying for a win directly against the hero players. Streamlined rules allow you to jump into the game quickly, and the miniatures are nicely detailed (though painters will lament flash and some random imperfections). The insert in this box is amongst the worst I've seen; once the map tiles were punched, it was nearly impossible to return everything to the box. I've had success playing the game solo, though you lose the surprise elements from the Overlord. Polished, intutive, replayable and benefitting from strong publisher support, you can't go wrong with Descent as the dungeon-delving representative in your collection.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
My new very favorite game! I'm even painting the miniatures!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
First Impression: This is a simple dungeon crawler with little to no bookkeeping. The components are top-notch FFG and the scenarios are really thematic. The minis are begging to be painted. Each character seems to play differently, with variable powers and abilities. 

10 Plays: The campaign structure and character progression in the game are compelling. Scenario balance seems to suffer somewhat with fewer characters and I believe the sweet spot would be a party of 4. The rushed feeling of the built-in timer mechanisms is beginning to feel tiring.

RATING: 3

Hopefully FFG will create another DOOM board game when DOOM 4 is released. DOOM was so much better than Descent 1.0 and it is even better than Descent 2.0. Another fail for me...

RATING: 8
I haven't played the 1st Edition of Descent, so I can not comment on the differences. I enjoy 2nd edition very much, although it does have its flaws. I do find the game very intense, the "rush" aspect of it often emphasizes the importance of every decision you make in the game, and there is lots of back and forth discussion among players about tactics and what to do.  The monster and heroes are interesting and the various effects and special attacks they can make add great flavour to the game.

I find the game somewhat annoyingly imbalanced at times - but this opinion could change, as I get to know the game a bit better and thus understand tactics and proper decision making. Our group is 4 players - 1 overlord and 3 heroes.  We lost three quests in a row at the end of Act 1 and the Interlude, seemingly because of just overwhelming odds, which caused one of our hero group to refuse to play ever again.  We continued into Act 2 and I kept playing his character, and we bought a powerful item, one a quest, and then picked up two Relics, winning the next two quests. The last two Act 2 quests we played were VERY close calls - which made for very exciting gameplay.

Because it's an FFG game, all the components are top notch. However, it really annoys me that they did not include miniatures for the lieutenants. This is part of their business model - they go on to sell pewter miniatures from their website for $10 each.  I know making molds for figures is very expensive... but I really wish they had included them.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Look, there's a lot to like about Descent 2.0: the miniatures are awesome, the character abilities and items are great, and the episodic play makes an epic game fit into nice bite-size chunks. But despite appearances this is not a "dungeon crawl" game, this is a tactical skirmish type thing, and in my experience, it's a pretty unbalanced one. True, some of our games came down to the wire, but just as many turned out to be disappointing blowouts. As the overlord, it's not fun seeing the heroes cripple your chances of winning before you've even taken a turn; likewise as the heroes, having a few dice rolls make an encounter obviously unwinnable really sucks the fun out. Both of these things happened across multiple playthroughs. The fact that so much of the game revolves around blocking movement rather than fighting and exploring seems to run counter to the theme, and despite the designers' best attempts, it's still just not much fun being the overlord. I really wanted to like it, but Descent just doesn't really do it for me.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Pros: really quick to set up and play.  Some complain about the set-up time to play ratio, but the set-up seemed very quick to me relative to the play time.  The first scenario was done in about 45 minutes.

Played the first scenario, Fat Goblin (two sets of encounters) and The Cardinal of something (two sets of encounters.)

We won 4 out of 5 encounters (granted, the starter is a given) - we only rescued 1 bundle of crops in the first Fat Goblin encounter set (counting that as a loss), and the Overlord rescued 3, making the second portion of the Encounter that much more difficult.  We still won the 2nd portion, but just by a single turn... Squib? would have been off the board on the OL's next turn.

We played a total of 6-7 hours of Descent.  In that amount of time, I'd rather play one or two 3-4 hour games of (properly modded) Doom.  During some of that time playing Descent, I simply stopped caring.  However, I was engaged at other times.

I did not like how in first Fat Goblin encounter set and in the 2nd Cardinal encounter set, you start with big fat creatures blocking your way!  Even in the very first encounter, we were quickly blocked by giant 4x4 monsters preventing our passage.  Makes me wish I had that Scout character which could walk through enemies!  They're just walls of meat you have to mow down!  Fortunately, in the 2nd case, Hugo the Glorious's special made quick work of one of the blocking golems.

Also, given the map sizes, I felt like there weren't very decisions you could make relative to Doom was reduced, compared to a sprawling map of Doom.

Respawning monsters also left me feeling a bit cold.

The encounters also feel rather disconnected, and doing well in the previous encounter can have a big effect on the following encounter.  In the Cardinal encounters, during the first one, the Heroes did very well and the OL only resurrected one zombie.  If he had resurrected 3 of 4 (rather than 1), the Heroes would have been much more greatly pressed for time during the 2nd Encounter and would've lost.  The fact that doing poorly in one encounter means you do poorly in the next seems a bit odd, given that they could easily be played in different sessions and didn't feel THAT connected.

In sum, Descent is a great bite-size adventure, especially if the heroes like to win.  Have an hour and a half?  You can totally play Descent 2nd Edition.

However, I'd rather play Doom - it's more interesting and tense throughout, whereas Descent felt like your decisions didn't really matter in some cases because there was only one or two real choices.

RATING: 6.5
More streamlined rules than the original.  Better balance in the campaign (with much less runaway leader problem) than Road to Legend.  One-shots and the campaign have much shorter playtime than their first edition counterparts.

The downsides compared to first edition that I can see:
The characters don't progress in the middle of quests.  If you're normally interested in one-shots and you like the idea of your heroes gaining power through the quest, this will be a *big* problem for you.
Seems less replayable than the original, and definitely less amusement/$ spent than the original.

I still like it better, but you do have to keep these in mind.

RATING: 9
When I was 12 my mum bought me a copy of Hero Quest, and we spent one night playing through it as a family. It sticks out as one of the most fun evenings that I can remember at that age, but its the only time I can actually remember playing the game in full.

I have a vague recollection of getting it out again to try and teach my friend, who became instantly disenchanted with it because I was insistent that when I played a certain card that I'd dealt for myself, it meant that his character instantly died.

Anyway, here I am in my 30s and looking for that 'dungeon adventure' type experience once more. A quick peek at eBay tells me that my near mint copy of Hero Quest - played properly only once, and played like a spanner perhaps once or twice more - was worth the better side of £100.... Excellent, I'm so glad I've still got it.

A brief phone call to my mother leaves me lost for words, as she ever so calmly advises me that most of my old toys went to charity. I don't talk to my mother any more.

So, where now? I'm not forking out silly money for a board game, but there's a certain itch that is becoming more and more prevalent and uncomfortable.

I REALLY REALLY WANT TO KILL A DRAGON!!!

Fast forward some extensive internet searching and I get the distinct impression that Descent SE is the way forward. 

Too bloody right it is - Dragons aplenty, magic coming out of your ears, and more goblins than you can shake a stick at.

If I were to give it one criticism, maybe its a bit too imbalanced in favour of the heroes. That said, the Overlord is a great role to play for the evening..... Lots of MWAHAHAHAs and such.

RATING: 8.5
Having played the 1st edition only about 6 times, I'll leave the comparisons to the experts.
This game is a solid try to have an RPG boardgame without too much a fuss. It is enjoyable, competitive and FUN.
The heroes have to be careful to gather the searches whenever given the chance though, to be up to par with the Overlord.
What I didn't like is the Line of Sight rules, since everyone usually has LoS to their targets, thus eliminating a tactical perspective of the game. Also, Large Monster Movement. Whereas a singe large monster can block to the path to all heroes, one hero cannot block the path to monsters. Tactically, that doesn't allow the Knight/Berserkers to act as "tanks" and maybe protect weaker characters in various situations. 
I'd like to see FFG being more active in answering the numerous questions of gamers.
All in all, a very entertaining game with an interesting campaign system.

RATING: 6.5
It's a nice streamlined campaign dungeon crawler. The game flows usually quickly, but since positioning and generally tactics are very important it may suffer from some necessary slow downs.

The campaign works well in general, but still there are major balancing issues between missions, overlord/heroes and importance of heroes for the party. Since descent's design is rather open and gravitates towards flashy, unique abilities, this has to be expected to a certain degree. 

On one side it does offer room for heroic deeds and devastating acts of malice, on the other it may result in some very one-sided, unexciting missions!

While I appreciate the design in general I still think it has some problems, which must be mediated by the group itself, which has to take care that balance doesn't completely fall apart.

RATING: 10
From HeroQuest as a kid until now, I've always loved a good dungeon crawl board game.  And I think this is as good as the genre has ever been.  One thing to be clear on before you get into this is that it doesn't have the feeling of exploration that Hero Quest or Descent 1st edition gives you.  This is a tactical fantasy miniatures game.  You know the goals as soon as the encounter is set up and it's basically a small tactical battle.  I know some people don't like 2nd Ed and the lack of exploration is the root of all their problems with the game.  The thing is, exploring rooms and killing stuff and collecting treasure gets old after a while if there isn't a strong story linking it all together.  Hero Quest (as much as I love it) and Descent 1st Ed. suffer from this sameyness.  In fact no board game has succeeded in giving a dungeon crawl feel along with a strong story- for that you need to play D&D.  Another thing 2nd has going over 1st edition is that this is so much more streamlined and easy to play than the bloated sessions of 1st Ed which basically ended up going nowhere anyway.  Anyway, enough comparisons.  This game has so many diverse scenarios and is rich with so much material, campaigns, and monsters, heroes, classes, etc.  You could play forever and never do the same thing.  And with a little imagination you can really get into the game and your character.  And there is a sense of discovery and story finding new equipment, encountering new monsters, and when each quest is set up, etc.  Combat is varied and fun with lots of special abilities but it doesn't bog down. Components are stellar.  Great game.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
After one play, the first word that comes to mind is "streamlined."  It's a dungeon crawl where the heroes are competing against a single Overlord.  Each player has one character and a few items.  There is a chance to get more (temporary) items during the quest which may make things a little more to manage, but I don't think it'll be too bad.  

Players' turns are smooth.  Play start of round actions, then take 2 actions (move ,attack, rest, use cards, ...).  Components are very good.  I liked the detail on the miniatures and the graphics on the board (modular for different dungeons and made of thick interlocking cardboard).  Components to keep track of are health, stamina (used to do extra stuff), the miniature on the board, player mat, and a few face up cards.  Nothing overwhelming and easy to teach.

I'm always impressed with games that take a long time but don't feel like they take a long time.  18XX often epitomizes that for me.  I had the same feeling with Descent.  We were eating and explaining and it was a teaching game, but I was still surprised to find that we spent 2 hours at it.  Didn't feel long at all.

I'm not sure if it's something I'd want to play every week, but it might be.  Both last night on the ride home and this morning, I've been trying to think how the Overlord could possibly win the quest.  For me, that's always a sign of a good game.

Although the first quest was easy, I can see the opportunities for complexity and I'm looking forward to exploring it.

Initial Rating: solid 7 with possible upward mobility.

RATING: 7.5
Es un juego temático de aventuras. Es la segunda edición, en la que se supone que han variado algunas mecánicas y en general han simplificado el juego, para que dure menos tiempo. Es bastante entretenido, y permite jugar campañas, encadenando escenarios. El escenario siguiente depende de cómo te fuera en el actual. Es un juego muy "cinematográfico". Cada partida es como una parte de una película (la peli sería la campaña entera).

Hay mucha variedad de personajes, cada uno con sus características propias. Es cooperativo, de 2 a 5 jugadores, y con duración variable, en función del escenario. En un escenario normal (sólo he jugado el de introducción) hay dos partes. La segunda parte también se configura según acabase la primrea. A priori parece tremendamente rejugable.

Recomendado para aquellos que disfruten con juegos que cuentan una historia.

RATING: 6
I am a HUGE fan of Descent 1 (i gave it a 10), and our gaming group aswell.
Descent 2 is a perfect example of a game that was streamlined to death.
With the change in mechanics most of the overlord objectives in the different quests comes down to delaying the heroes for as long as possible to win the game.
prolonging the game for as long as possible is such a horrible game mechanic and makes no fun at all!

We have rather strong players in our group including the overlord(s). The game is still "balanced", in a sence  that the heroes win about 50% of the game. I say this because even though the overlord succeeds in delaying the heroes, the game often (not always) is balanced by a blunt and boring game mechanic that often comes down to an almost random win of ether side at the end of the quest. 

The positive aspects of the game is the rather short playtime of about 3 hours per quest on one hand and the flawless integration of the campaign on the other.
in every other aspect i would recommend to buy the first edition. It may tay take up to 8 hours per quest and there is only a horrible campaign mechanic which came through an expansion, but it is also epic, the heroes many divers action possibilitys influence the outcome ultimatly and at the end of the day it is just way more fun!

I still have hope for the game anyways. Descent 1 became the game it is now because of its marvelous expansions which enriched the gameplay with many game mechanics. Descent 2 has yet to get an expansion which fullfills the same needs. Until then i stay disappointed and the game will hit the table every month or so in the hope, that the next quest will better than the last..

/edit after 4 years: FFG screwed up, descent is dead

RATING: 7.2
Playtest the game with 4 other players.  Two plays, finished 4 encounters, 1 basic (2 encounters) and 1 expert (2 encounters) games.

I played with Ranger in the first basic game and Magic User in the secound game.

Expert game is much interesting than the basic game because there are more options.

The basic game seems a bit dry and too simple.

I don't like the large size monster movement rule but I know it is designed for speed up the game (shorter the playing time).  The line-of-sight rule is another unusual rule I don't like so much.  Again it is designed on purpose to shorten the playing time.

Descent is a solid game with fast play rule and many options to keep your interest in the game.  The story behind each encounter is like a rpg adventure which I like the most.

+/- Kampagne könnte ganz Nett sein

+ Balance scheint halbwegs zu stimmen
+ Erfahrungssystem

2. Spiel Kampagnenneustart:
Maskenball mit 2 Helden = lächerlich.
Maximal 2 Gäste kann man retten. Würfelwurf erbrachte eine 3 und somit eine Niederlage für die Helden. Das 1. Szenario hätte man sich somit schenken können.

7->6.5
- Spawnmechanik: Mist- warum sollte der OL jemals kleine Monster in einer freien Gruppe einsetzen. Vor seinem Spielzug erhält er meistens Verstärkung was die Helden dazu zwingt Monster zu Betäuben, Lähmen oder sonstiges.
- Bewegungssystem von großen Monstern: Der größte Witz- Ein Drachen mit Bewegung 4 kann ohne  weiteres in einem Zug 7+ Felder bewegt werden...schneller als jedes kleine Monster.
-Wie in Descent 1 unausgewogene Szenarien. Manche Szene 2 Szenarien sind für Helden oder Ol praktisch nicht mehr zu gewinnen wenn Szene 1 nicht geschafft wurde.
Wie "balanciert die Szenarien sieht erkennt man Eindrucksvoll an den ganzen Änderungen des FAQ



Nach einer fast abgeschlossenen Kampagne.
Bullshit. Man fragt sich warum es mehrere Szenen gibt. Letztlich läuft jedes Spiel auf ein Wettrennen hin in dem die Helden mit 3-4 Schätzen rauskommen und der OL in der 2. Szene fast alle Karten auf der Hand hat.
Descent 2 ist kein totaler Fehlschlag, für ne kurze Partie zwischendrin ist es ok. Wann immer aber Zeit ist würde ich Descent 1 jederzeit vorziehen.

Nach der Kampagne: 
Overlord machte den Fehler dass er zwischen den einzelnen Szenen immer 4 Karten zusätzlich zog, dadurch wurde es für die Heldengruppe extrem schwer da der OL oftmals die Karten die er in der vorrunde Spielte sofort wieder auf die Hand bekam. RATING: 8.5
I wanted an RPG that harkens back to the joys I used to experience during games of Hero Quest; instead, I ended up with Shining Force on a board.  Is this a bad thing?  Definitely not, though it did through me for a loop for the beginning.  

Let me reiterate:  TACTICAL game with rpg elements.  TACTICAL, not a dungeon delving RPG.

Possibly one of the best part about this game is that the Overlord has a clear objective:  To WIN.  There's none of this "tell a good story, and make sure the heroes have fun on their path to victory" crap.  Plain, simple, WIN.

Descent is also deceptively simple.  An initial read-through of the instructions feels straight-forward enough, but it's not until after you've played a few Quests that you realize you didn't know half of what you thought you knew.  True to Fantasy Flight's standards, words mean everything.  Terms such as "target space" do not mean the same as "target empty space" (For clarification, a character may only target a space if it is occupied by an enemy figure).  As such, Descent presents a steep learning curve, but once achieved, it is well worth the time and energy spent to play it.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Edge Entertainment
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
Mayday Mini USA, FF Green, UltraPro 4"x6"
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Out of the box, this is overall a cleaner and much more playable version of v1. Much of the fat and fiddliness has been stripped away. Despite the cleanup, though, it did take a couple steps backward.

In particular - scenario play in this game is lackluster. It's still fun, turn to turn, but it doesn't have the sweeping epic progression. And I really, really miss that.

Usually I find it more fulfilling to sit down and play 1 scenario in Descent v1 in 2.5-3 hrs than I do to play a few quests in v2 in the same amount of time.

Campaign play is what this version is all about. It's been seriously streamlined from Road to Legend. It's still not perfect, but if you want a dungeon crawler you can play a campaign of I haven't found a better one.

The variety of and upgrade path for the heroes, due to having fixed decks this time around, feels restrictive compared to v1. It works BETTER for campaign play this way, but I feel like it made this game a little less replayable.

The Overlord also gets less choices, but honestly, it does get things going a lot faster and not having to bother with currency refreshing.

The new hero respawn system is an improvement for both the heroes and the Overlord. Not having to run from a glyph back to your group is nice, and if the Overlord gets you in the right spot he can kill you over and over.

The mixup of dice is also simpler so the rolls end up feeling pretty... samey and less interesting. But again, streamlined.

Honestly I can forgive all these little things. Descent v1 might have been more interesting but it was a lot more fiddly. However there is one huge glaring issue I have with this game:

The new monster spawning system is fucked. It's nice that the Overlord doesn't have to rely on cards to spawn monsters, but it's utterly unforgivable that the Overlord can, for no cost, re-spawn a gigantic creature you just spent turns and turns taking down IMMEDIATELY ON THE NEXT TURN. What the fuck is this about? It's bullshit for the heroes and it's boring if you're the Overlord. No choice, no tension. Blah.

Despite that huge issue, I still have lots of fun whenever we play. Turn to turn, this is a fun system and is the best dungeon crawler I've found to date. Here's to hoping for an improved v3, I guess.

RATING: 6.5
Yay, I finally got to play this dungeon crawler! I’m a hardcore eurogamer, but I’m also an “omnigamer”; I am willing to play every single game at least once. While eurogames are still by far my favorite genre, I also sort of enjoy light wargames and games which mix euroelements with ameritrash (such as, hm, Survive Escape from Atlantis, Eclipse, Claustrophobia, Kemet etc.) Descent is definitely a game in the ameritrash genre, and while I find it okay, it also showed me what I already knew; that eurogames are, in my personal book, the better games. 

I don’t really care about the theme or storytelling elements in games, and I hate random dice rolls, so Descent already was at a disadvantage before I even tried it out! After my 2-3 plays of it (two scenarios, the second being divided into two), I must say that it was an enjoyable experience which I will play again if asked. I think the reason is that, despite the things I don’t like, there is sort of a puzzle to solve with your friends, just like there is in a coop game – and I love coops. It was good fun to cooperate with three other players against the dungeon master. Also, there was actually a fair bit of strategy involved and some euro-y elements. You take two actions out of 7-8 or so each turn and need to choose those which best handles the specific situation. I like how the scenarios evolve and your character evolves too and gets extra abilities etc. The various characters feel different due to their special powers and the items they carry. 

Descent played very well with 4 heroes, and it played in a reasonable amount of time which is quite important to me – had the game taken 4 hours (like its predecessor) I am sure I would never play it again, but as our scenarios only took about 1-2 hours each, I’m definitely willing to play it again as long as I’m one of the heroes; I know I won’t enjoy the game as much if playing the dungeon master. All in all, Descent was a positive surprise and a game which, much to my surprise, I would play again a couple of times.

To sum it up, truly excellent production with fun game play and alot of thing to choose as both the heroes and the overlord but with a kinda bad story.

RATING: 10
One of the best games I've ever played, if not my #1 game.  Many play this as an Ameritrash game, but I play it as a Euro. It allows you to play it either way! The amount of pre-planning that goes into a quest is off the charts, and you get rewarded for it. Every turn and decision is extremely tense with the right group. The replayability is also insane due to the sheer number of ways to play: one-shot quests, many official campaigns, fan-made campaigns and one-shots, make your own quests/campaigns with FFG's editor, co-op mode with the Road to Legend app, and a crazy number of expansions. Even with the complexity and depth that this game offers, FFG managed to create an incredibly balanced experience at the 5 player count. The only complaint I have about this game is the dice rolls and the LOS rules are a bit wonky, otherwise it's perfect.

RATING: 6
Over the course of a year, four friends and I played through the base campaign of Descent 2ED with me as Overlord. All of us were new to Descent. While I appreciate aspects of the game design, I just didn't have that much fun playing it. About the first 1/3 of the campaign I was a friendly Overlord that avoided using cheap feeling tactics. While the heroes seemed to have fun, it felt a little boring, and I also realized if I didn't slow them down there would be a snowball effect with them getting good gear and becoming unstoppable.

From then on I started playing as Mean Overlord, and while the heroes lost most of the missions after that and ultimately the campaign, they didn't seem to have that much fun and I was stressed out trying to maximize my strategy by keeping track of everything (which also became more and more difficult as they gained new equipment and abilities). 

One technique I often used was to horde Overlord cards and then unleash them all at once to pull off some crazy combo and win in a way that didn't make much sense thematically. While effective, that wasn't very fun compared to if I had been using my Overlord abilities more sporadically. Often, it felt like if I won a mission, there wasn't anything they could have done to stop me, and vice-versa if they won.

I also often used the same monsters to maximize my chances when a more even representation of the different monsters would have been more interesting.

The missions tended to last too long for my tastes, even when I set the game up ahead of time. Whereas 2-3 hours would have been nice, it often took us around 4 hours just to get through a two-part mission.

While I think there were a lot of good design decisions (such as simplifying line of sight), one design decision I don't agree with was for the winner of each mission to be more rewarded than the loser and also get to choose what the next mission would be.

Finally, the back story and intro/outro descriptions were really poorly written for people who weren't previously familiar with the universe Descent takes place in. Half the time we were confused why the missions were even taking place.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
Keep


6/2/2012 - Having played the quest almost half way through with two players (myself and Tom) with Tom playing 3 heroes, I have decided to rate this game as a 9.5. The gaming experience is excellent - it is so good that I can forgive the lack of clarity in some instances in the rules and the FAQ. 

So far in the quest as the overlord I lost the introduction quest, and I lost the first Act 1 quest we played (Death on the Wing). While Death on the Wing was not a total wipeout, victory for the heroes was reasonably comfortable. After this I changed strategy for the next quest (A Fat Goblin) and hoarded my cards from the first encounter, which paid off in the second encounter. The last quest played was the Masquerade Ball. I attempted to hoard my cards from the first encounter but in the end I think I started with 7 of my 15 cards in the second encounter after drawing. This was still enough to delay the heroes on the first move although I still didn't have everything I would have liked to throw at them early. A few good rolls however meant Lady Farrow was through a couple of doors fairly quickly. This encounter was the closest we have played so far. With Lady Farrow having 4 life left and at the last door (with 3 heroes bearing down on me). I had to escape through the door with the last attribute test. The roll worked, however it could have easily gone the other way. One more turn for the heroes and it was lights out. While I won this scenario, I am concerned that the benefit gained from winning was not that great, as the heroes picked up a large number of items while travelling and searching, meaning they had a bonanza at the shop. I fear this may have a significant benefit for them next quest, but we shall see.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
Really enjoying this game.  While it is made for 5 players max, I have been easily able to adapt it to a fun 7-9 player action.  

This game takes everything I loved about HeroQuest and builds on it.  This creates a fun linear campaign like HQ, but also allows for single quest or battle play through for those rainy day afternoons with some Noobs.  The expanded battle system and mechanics can be a bit tricky at the start, but iron themselves out easily as users get used to the features of the game.

The PROS:

Great for 2-9 players with some extended scaling and innovation.

Easy to learn and play

Characters stand alone and work well together at the same time.  Allows for multiple play styles to team up together.

The CONS:  

Only designed for 4 players max, and does take some creativity to scale for more players.

A bit heavy on rules for the non-gamer

Long sessions to accomplish single quests.

RATING: 7.5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.2
Though its predecessor was apparently a beast to play, Descent 2.0 has very intuitive rules once you get over all the small print in the rules booklet. It’s the ne plus ultra of dungeon crawlers: nice components, including great monster miniatures, a unique way of using dice, and a fun campaign system that has just enough complexity to make it interesting. One player plays the Dark Overlord, the rest individual warriors, mages, priests or rogues. I suggest watching a good “how to play” video first (e.g. like Rodney Smith’s), then looking up specific rules as needed. Other than the small print in the rules (a problem with a lot of Fantasy Flight games), my only real criticisms are (a) the uselessness of the cardboard insert, which prevents all the components fitting in the box (I cut it up and used the pieces to make two smaller boxes that fit everything in it nicely), and (b) the somewhat anti-thematic “no hero death” rule, which admittedly avoids player elimination. Instead of the fear of death motivating players, it’s time: each encounter makes the heroes act quickly before the Overlord achieves his objective.

RATING: 5
One player takes on the role of Overlord, and tries to defeat the other players, the heroes, in a fantasy dungeon skirmish quest.  Simple game, where players take turns activating their heroes (or monster groups), and move, fight, search, rest and quest their way through the scenario. Combat is clean, game play is brisk, and the scenarios are a perfect length.  The quest can be played one off, or as part of a linked campaign.  The components are excellent, and offers good, surprisingly tactical game play.  A vast improvement over the beast that was first edition Descent. Well done, if a bit soulless and one dimensional.

RATING: 7
I believe that DOOM is the best iteration of this system.  DOOM gets you to the good stuff (tactical combat, special abilities, fun dice resolution system) with the least amount of hassle.  The hook of Descent 2 for me is the campaign system.  In concept, it is cool that the heroes and the overlord can level up.  However, the two sides are leveling up more or less equally, so the "leveling up" really just means that everyone rolls more dice and can take more damage.  It is not like the leveling up in say, Runebound, where you have to judge when you are powerful enough to take on greater challenges.  In D2, you do a series of missions at level 1, then a series at level 2, which is similar except everyone rolls twice as many dice.   This is underwhelming to me.  However, I do like how the outcome of each encounter affects how following ones unfold.  The campaign is suitably epic, but it is a commitment of quite a few game nights.  

Personally, I'd prefer to just get in and out with DOOM for the combat, play Mansions of Madness for the narrative, or Earth Reborn for a more intricate tactical experience.  D2 falls through these cracks for me, and is therefore getting Jones ruled off my shelves.  I would still very happily play D2 anytime, though.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
(Unchanged from 7 1st ed)
So far, we've played 2 incomplete campaigns of Descent 2nd, as well as the full co-op campaign.  In general, I like it more than the 1st edition, but it has introduced new problems as much as it's fixed things.  

So, the good: there's been a crapload of simplification.  This has hit pretty much everywhere.  Special skills and abilities are gone, replaced with a new class system.   The whole separation between town & dungeon (teleporting between them) has been done away with.  Threat and special spawning is gone, though the overlord's deck and its customization remains.  The campaign's been trimmed down, so you no longer wander about freely on this huge map, but instead proceed along a branching storyline, hitting specific story missions and some side ones.  The overlord similarly doesn't have to worry about sieging towns and moving his lieutenants, but just winning the missions he's fighting (and still upgrading his guys).  I could go on, but they've streamlined so much.

There's new ideas, too!  Your characters have a special ability and an archetype, and you then select a specific class from within that archetype.  The class determines what kinds of things your guy will be doing.  For instance: If you're a blue (support) type, you could be an alchemist tossing potions at people, or a bard buffing your team with passive songs, or a more traditional cleric-type healer.  If you're a yellow (caster) type, you could become a necromancer and move your skeleton figure around attacking things, or you could be a geomancer who kind of does similarly but with summoned stones, or the more classic runemaster who just blasts things with magic.  Of course, only so many of these classes are in the base game, so if you want more choices, you'll need to invest in the expansions.  
Once you've picked your class, that sets up your base ability that you tend to build off of, as well as starting equipment.  As you level up, you get exp, which you can spend to unlock more abilities (there are a couple each of 1-, 2-, and 3-cost skills).  Some of them make your base skill stronger in some way, others give you new attacks and such.

The campaign is now built into the game, instead of being an expansion add-on, which is a big change.

Now the not-so-good.  There are just a couple things from the old version that I miss.  Being able to use the special orders was situational, but often very powerful.  Now the ability to react during the enemy's turn (or even defend) is gone.

With the above changes has come one regarding hero deaths.  Instead of respawning back in town, and the overlord being rewarded with points, your figure is KOed on the space it was occupying.  You can take your entire next turn to stand back up, or an adjacent friend can take one action to revive you.  This is all fine.  However, when you stand back up, you roll 2 red dice and get back that much HP & stamina.  This is usually around 5 HP (as low as 3 or as much as 6), and 1 stamina (if you're lucky).  That's fine in the early game, but woefully inadequate later on.  Instead of threat or points, the overlord gets to draw a card when he KOs someone.  So, in later missions, if a weaker or weakened hero is ever even slightly exposed, they can get mobbed with enemies and killed in short order.  Then, they're left with no option but to stand back up, get killed, stand back up, over and over.  This is really not fun, nor is it good design.
Which leads me on to the next point about balance.  Some of the hero classes I talked about above seem crappy, but are actually quite useful in practice (the bard, for instance).  Others seem crappy because they are.  There's a big difference in power between some of the class types.  
As noted earlier, the overlord has deck specializations he can go into, and these have similar disparities.  Traps seem pretty lackluster, while the weapon mastery (I forget what it's really called) has some crazy combos in it.  For instance, you can put a couple of cards in your deck that add extra damage to your monsters' attack rolls.  S, our overlord, quickly noticed that these return to your hand if you spend a surge during the attack, and there's another card that adds a surge to your attack..  So, after a few turns of drawing (the OL's deck is only 20 cards), he can begin spamming this card that gives [i]every[/i] monster attack 2 extra dmg, and he'll never lose it unless he gets a series of spectacularly bad die rolls.  2 may not sound like much, but trust me, its hard to stay alive under that kind of assault.

Oh, and while I'm thinking about it, there's some really perverse incentives created by the monster spawning system.  The old one had you running around trying to cover all the sight lines, while the new one has defined spawn points.  But the old one also had a cost to respawn things, while the new one gives the OL one every turn, for free.  So in a bizarre way, you don't always want to kill the monsters.  If it's your last character's turn, you've just come into this new area and none of the monsters in it have died yet, plus everyone else has just spent time moving/healing or tried to attack but only wounded things, there's zero point in you trying to kill something.  If you do, it'll just come back to life at the end of the round, at full health.  If you can't wipe all of them out in one go, it might be better to wound/incapacitate and try it next turn.  This is especially true if the monster group is one of the larger, tougher ones, and doubly so if you're killing off a red one.  (Monster groups have 1 or more white basic type and a red one with better stats.  The OL gets to bring back any one thing he wants, so he'll always choose the red one of the strongest group he's got.)  I really feel like there should be some kind of cooldown on these things, like each individual figure has to sit out a turn before respawning, or a red one costs more to bring back than a white.  Conversely, perhaps the OL should get some compensation if the heroes haven't killed anything and he's entitled to a respawn, but everything's still on the map.  

They've completely messed with the way that missions work, and how they're balanced.  In some ways this is an improvement, since you always have story-specific goals and it's not just a grind-fest of trying to get as many hero kills as possible while delaying their advance as long as possible.  However, they've replaced the effective timer that was threat and monster spawning (the longer you took, the more you'd have to fight) with these artificial timers.  I wouldn't mind as much, except [i]every single mission[/i] has one.  It's always "you have 3 turns to get across the bridge", or "you have to save this guy before he's eaten by zombies", or "you've got to make it to the treasure before the villain does".  There's always some type of race, even if the timer isn't fixed and explicit.  Worse, it's clear that some of these weren't very well-considered.  Time and again, we've run into situations where the overlord can accomplish his goal easily, whether by playing a card that gets him an extra move or simply by virtue of the scenario design.  Now, the heroes can move to block these efforts (in some cases), or exert themselves to try and win in a hurry, but they have to know what they're doing.  Going into a scenario blind, with just the instructions from the manual, it's really tough to anticipate these twists and win.  
Worse, some of them are completely luck-based.  There are quite a number where you have to find a needle in a haystack, or at least the right token out of a facedown set of 6, or the right room of 3 possible places something might be hidden.  If you get lucky and it's in the first place you look, you're set up to win, but if it's in the last place, you're pretty much screwed.  
Other scenarios revolve around making attribute tests to do things, and if you've got the wrong mix of heroes or just get unlucky in your rolls, you can forget about winning.
Now, I will say that there are some of these that we've looked at and said "this is impossible",  but it's turned out to be very close.  So maybe there are times when my judgement of the balance is off.  Or perhaps we just got lucky to make it that far.  There have also been scenarios where we've felt the same way and it's turned out exactly as we predicted, an easy waltz to the win for the overlord with nothing we could do to stop it.
The final point I'll make is that most of the missions are designed in 2 parts.  In the first, you're introduced to the adventure and make it into the area to confront the first challenge.  Whichever side wins the first half doesn't get a real reward, instead they get a bonus or penalty when it comes to the 2nd half of the mission.  The first halves may also be the most skewed, in my experience.  So if you can't beat the odds in the 1st part, you'll be facing even steeper ones in the 2nd.

We have had successful, contested, interesting campaigns despite these issues.  But we have also had at least one that turned into a downward spiral for  the heroes.  If the overlord wins, he's that much more likely to keep winning.  And although both sides get Exp (&/or money) regardless of the outcome, the winner will get more, and be in a stronger position next time.  So, I think if the OL plays to win and is cutthroat about it, and the heroes don't know the missions in advance, you can easily slip into that death funnel without realizing it.(Unchanged from 7 1st ed)

RATING: 5.47
The game is beautiful, miniatures are, like in many other FFG games, awesomely done and modular board components are perfect. However, the game itself is too simple and too shallow and out of the place. Some rules are simply ridiculous (e.g. movement or line of sight) and game can easily become too meta for my taste. And lieutenants are not even figures but mere tokens. They can be bought for $10 a piece. 
What could have been great table top RPG turned to be dice rolling hack 'n' slash with zero role playing. Something like D&D4e. And it's Terrinoth again. Give up FFG, Terrinoth will never be a thing! On other side, it's tons of fun for ten-year-olds.
To sum up: I am really happy to own this game as components themselves are worth it. It is an awesome tool for your own RPG campaign design in any slightly more serious setting.

[b]Edit:[/b] Downgraded from 6.0 to 5.3 due to very low replayability and lack of motivation to acquire expansions. Just no.
[b]Update:[/b] Rating modified according to a custom rating system.

[b]Favorite mechanism:[/b] None.

RATING: 7.5
If you've got any sort of vague interest in role playing in dungeons and dragons or pathfinder - do yourself a favour and pick up Descent 2 instead. You'll save a bucket of money, and probably have more fun with this anyhow.

Descent is a stripped down dungeon crawling rpg crammed into a board game. Stripped down, because the rules are so simple they just make sense. This is a crowning achievement for any board game.

What makes Descent special? The scenario book.
Every scenario is a brilliantly creative little adventure that plays out in about an hour, maybe an hour and a half. Including explaining the rules to new comers. Unbelievable hey?

The sorts of things that happen during your scenarios:
-The heroes are trying to run through a canyon, while the overlord rains boulders down to try and hem them in
-The heroes are at a masquerade ball, trying to uncover cultists while escorting guests to safety
-The overlord is trying to march his necro-dragon across the bridge, before the heroes can destroy his phylactery

Very, very rarely does a scenario boil down to "kill everything that moves, get a key."

Super solid game, recommended. I need to play more of this.

Edit 16/12/2015
Downrated to 8.0
We've been playing through the campaign included in the base box. Game has a severe issue with 'Quarterbacking' - dominant players effectively taking other players turns away. Mission variety is good, but not balanced - there are some missions that are clearly unwinnable from one side or the other. Balance for competitive play is difficult. We've house ruled in the LoS rules from Imperial Assault (one corner from attacker to two corners of defender) and that's been working well.
Overall this is still a great game. However I'm a bit burnt out from it, and it is starting to feel repetitive.

RATING: 4
More streamlined and generally better than the first [thing=17226]Descent[/thing]. Cool miniatures. Actually playing the game is fun, but setup can take really long. You need a dungeon master who is well prepared and (optimally) sets up the game in advance.

I found out that I'm not a player who enjoys campaign style games. The reason being that you need to play the game with the same group of people. I played the game quite a few times with 3 players and managed to get a level 8 character. But every time you play with a different group of players, you have to start all over again. Especially when you have new players, you end up playing the same scenarios multiple times and I didn't find this exciting at all.

Another problem with the game I had was that while I generally really like when you're able to level up, I didn't really find the powers themselves that great. I never had the feeling that I want to buy them all but don't have enough experience points. Most of them are pretty mediocre so saving up was always an easy choice.

Lastly, I absolutely hate the 'X' on the blue die. The blue die doesn't deal that much damage to begin with and having a critical miss side that cancels out all your other dice is infuriating. This means 1/6 of all your rolls will be misses and this isn't fun to me. They could have made it a '0' side, which would have been bad as well but you could still make the roll if you got lucky. Alternatively, they could have used a critical hit side as a counterbalance. This would have made the game more random but possibly more exciting.

In conclusion, I think it ultimately comes down to having the same group to play with regularely so you can really enjoy the campaign and all of the expansions. Because I think when you can minimize setup through proper preparation and have a fun crowd it overshadows the other downsides of the game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
My group and I played a yearlong Descent v1 campaign using Road to Legend. I went from loving the game to loathing it. The big problem with Descent v1 was how the Overlord would activate all his guys at once and pick off one guy (often strolling by others; [i]ta ta![/i]) until he was knocked out. Then repeat. This was THE way for the OL to play every scenario as it worked. And the downtime waiting for him to maximize his 10+ dudes and cards was annoying. It felt imbalanced in terms of both power and fun. Throw in lots of rules ambiguities and the fun got sucked out, and we simply ended things prematurely because nobody was having fun.

Then SW:IA comes along and, as wary as I am, I'm convinced to try it -- and lo and behold, I find it fixes just about everything I hated about Descent. The one-at-a-time monster group activation vs all-at-once is brilliant so turns go much more quickly and things feel much more balanced (no more monster parade of suffering 12 attacks before your next turn). We meet monthly for a 1vMany campaign. So, when Descent v2 was announced, one of my friends picks up and we start playing a coop campaign using the app. I’m in love again! 

But something is bothering me about these games. I leave my sessions fried after 4+ hours of parsing out permutations of movements and actions to find the best one because the result always comes down to the dice anyway. I don’t feel that the player agency is commensurate with the effort required. You can figure out the best order of moves all you want, but the dice will determine the story told. 

Furthermore, the key to the game is not exploring a dungeon but racing to get to the next phase of the mission. You don’t clear a room and loot the chest, you just kill what’s in the way and keep moving. The app has a built-in timer that lets you know you’re not progressing as quickly as you should and penalizes you for going too slow (we always go too slowly). It’s antithetical to how I want the experience to be. I want a dungeon crawl where I get to immerse myself in a story, explore a dungeon, and kill a bunch of monsters, not race to reach objective points.

Rating dropped from a 9 to a 5 after several plays. It's OK, but this is no longer the only game in town. I'm looking at Gloomhaven for a true dungeon crawl experience.

RATING: 5
Given the success of campaign games such as Pandemic legacy in out group a lot is expected of Descent, and I personally feel it sort of failed to deliver.

The app intergration for XCOM, and to a lesser extent Mansions of Madness (2nd ed.) are awesome as they teach you to play from scratch, however here we found the app teaches player who know how to play descent how to play descent with the app- a subtle distinction maybe- but we spent A LOT of time having to double check the rule book for clarifications- on the plus side the rulebook index is pretty good.

Maybe it was simply because it was the training/tutorial mission we played but I found it very simple with no room for tactics of strategy and incredibly easy, posing almost no challenge at all. Dissappointing :soblue:

RATING: 5
Spent too long trying to decide on the right rating for this game. The game itself is sound. I like the rules, i like the theme, the mechanics are excellent and playing any given turn is fun... But the quests/scenarios (I.e. the campaign) is HORRIBLY inbalanced. I've played games where I got to do NOTHING for turn after turn as the others players would kill all my pieces before my turn, and they'd then kill my reinforcements before I got to activate them. Later in the campaign the hero's have a near impossible task to survive, let alone complete a mission. After 3-4 month of play, the final battle pretty much then came down to a coin toss. Make the roll, Hero's win, fail to kill my monster and I win... on a single die roll.
I lost... and I think I had more fun than the hero players did... and I was misserable! I hope that FFG can fix the balance issues because I SO want this game to work. It should be a 9/10 rated game, but due to the balance issues it's a 3/10 (hence entering it as a 5 overall).

-Campaign mode that retains outcomes of each quest
-Character grow over time and can gain new equipment or abilities after each quest
-Use of unique dice in combat
-High quality components and well supported game

Cons
-Awkward respawning rules and lack of benefit for killing monsters leads to a more "run for the objective" style than the expected dungeon crawler/brawl
-Need to constantly rebuild the game map after every quest
-Very swingy campaign mode. Winners tend to win more as they get more bonuses after each victory.
-Combat is fully dice / luck
-Long down time for individual hero players (since Overlord has more to do).

RATING: 8
I like the archetypes and how you can choose from the two classes in each archetype. I like being able to equip weapons that provide you with different offensive/defensive options. I like that there is LOS. Having an Overlord control the events, traps, and monsters makes the game feel less mechanized than D&D:WoA. However, when playing it seems like an unfair advantage swings back and forth between the heroes and the Overlord, depending on which scenario you play. The other thing I wish Descent had was the randomly-generated dungeon mechanic that is in WoA. It adds to the feeling of exploration, instead of having the map already laid out before you. My gaming group is halfway through the campaign now and everyone is loving it. Going to have to get all of the expansions. I'd like to be able to play through the campaign again some time as one of the adventurers.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I appreciate the accessibility of this game and the design elements that respect the player's time in comparison to 1st edition where trips to the rulebook were much too frequent. Seemingly small things -- like the status effect cards with descriptions or explanations of monster abilities on the actual monster cards -- are welcome modifications that make the game easier to play.

On the other hand I think it cut too deeply into the Descent formula and made it unrecognizable as a [i]dungeon crawl[/i]. It's now mostly a tactical race, which isn't what I was initially looking for in Descent. I imagine the designers saw that the main complaint against first edition was the play time and therefore focused most of the revisions toward speeding the play. In doing this, they refined things more in the direction of Descent's predecessor [thing=10640]Doom[/thing]: a less interesting tactical rush system.

So first edition, especially [thing=29298][/thing], still has a definite place in my collection. I'll probably be getting rid of second edition soon. 

Edit: Sold. Boring.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
[b][i]Descent 2e[/i][/b] is perhaps the simplest complicated game I've ever played. By this I mean it isn't a simple game (it's got a lot of rules), but after you learn the game the rules fade into the background and you're left focusing on the characters and quest objectives. The great thing about this is you don't spend the game thinking things such as, "This figure has line of sight to these 4 squares with a 70% chance to hit." Instead, you automatically translate the game into narrative terms, "The goblin can see this entire corridor and will probably hit my weakly defended healer." This happens because the rules are clear cut, make sense, and are easy to remember.

The only two times I find myself wishing for a little extra complication (or at least something different) is when characters get knocked out (which is fairly mild in consequence) and when characters visit the store (I'd prefer equipment was limited by class, even though this would require an extensive table describing exactly who can equip what). In all other things, including the much maligned line of sight rules, I agree that FFG made the right calls to produce a fun, exciting, and tactical dungeon crawler.

One last item worthy of mention is the quest and character advancement system. Traveling, leveling, shopping, alternate Act 2 quests, etc. are all a total blast.

◊s◊

RATING: 5
Out of my entire collection, I have spent the most time with Descent 2.0

But not playing it...

Lemme explain.

I've wanted so terribly to love this game and for it to work with my group. I have proxy miniatures for the old monsters, all personally painted
But...
It just doesn't work for me. Every time I've played it, it's somewhat dull. It doesn't give me a D & D feel. No exploration. No fear of death by the heroes. Just not enough tension or choices.

It has become my nemisis.

Hopefully one day I will find a way to enjoy this game.
But saidly, it's seems as though I am just walking through a preset story that I can customize.

I can see this being a lot of fun with younger kids as myself being the overlord.

It gets a five cause of the components and the hope I can find a way to use it.

RATING: 8.5
Not your standard hack n’ slash dungeon crawler.  Nice blend of fantasy-style questing and character building with light elements of role-playing.  Descent can be played either as a 2-hour game or a 20-hour campaign (I prefer the campaign for the best immersion experience).  Objective-based quests make no two campaigns the same.  Interesting Overlord mechanics keep this game competitive and interactive for the game master (if you like being the game master that is).  Beautiful miniatures and modular map tiles.  For appreciation of what it truly is (a tactical skirmisher, NOT a classic dungeon crawler), IMO Descent 2nd edition is a fantasy-theme gamer's dream come true.  Check out Nerdook's and RedJak's variants for even more ways to play this game.  As of 2017 I owe all the expansions and hero & monster packs.

RATING: 8

RATING: 10
The game hits the right balance between story driven scenarios (each sensibly tuned to give the players a good impression of what the scene is supposed to be), tactical combat and various power richness.

Every hero class plays quite differently, but at the same time they all belong to one of four archetypes (Healer, Fighter, Mage, Rogue), giving flexibility, variety AND reliability. 

No scenario is even similar to the others, and playing the same scenario twice with different heroes and monsters feels like a totally new experience.

2 players with the full complements of 4 heroes gives a wonderful playing.

RATING: 8.7
Loads better than first edition. The most impressive part, I think, is how they shrunk the experience into bite-sized pieces while still keeping the balance interesting. Now, when you play, a very interesting thing happens: BOTH sides feel like the other side has a major advantage over them. And every few turns, you pull something off that alleviates that feeling for a turn. So you have this constant back-and-forth of having the upper hand. It's great! The missions are also way more dynamic now, the rules are simpler and more intuitive, they cut out a lot of unecessary mechanics. They basically fixed or improved everything.

RATING: 4
I was more excited about this game than any other, being a big fan of 1st edition, and in particular Road to Legends. We have played 2nd edition about half way through Act 2. I hate to say it, but I don't like this game, which is mind boggling in a way considering all the glowing reviews. I play with my wife, who considers 1st edition (RtL) to be one of her top three favorite games. I am playing the Overlord (as usual) with her as the heroes. I am massively pulling my punches, and I usually don't even play cards, and she is having a horrendous time winning any of the encounters. Combat plays almost no part if you want to be successful as the heroes, due to many of the encounters having time constraints, unfair Overlord advantages with abilities, and movement rules that can block areas so one side flat out can't complete an objective (I spend much of each game thinking that I'm glad I'm the Overlord as I would be frustrated as hell as the heroes). I think I'm going to shelf this one and see what expansions come out. Very important: This is NOT a more streamlined version of the original game. It is a different game all together, and we probably would have enjoyed it if it was marketed as such.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
It would be a bummer if the second edition was worse than the first one. Luckily this is an improvement in many regards. Right now, I just want to try out the campaign for the game.

[i]Easier, but not dumbed down[/i]
The game has in many ways been streamlined. Some has to do with better graphic design; like that there are not a ton of referencing the rule book for special abilities and reduced clutter on the board. There is also less bookkeeping and counting up in the game, with fewer dice, changed surge rules and easier overlord threat and deck management. All changes considered this makes the game not only easier to pick up for new players, but also more fast-paced with experienced ones.

[i]Shorter missions and slower progression[/i]
Missions are parted in usually two encounters where the first one is staging up how hard the second one is. The game is not just about killing the adventurers or the end boss anymore which is really refreshing. Some encounters are over in three turns which I find too short as they can be over before one of sides can really react, but you can’t longer go from newbie to superhero in one mission either. I don’t find it excusable that they don’t seem to be balanced with all number of players. This makes me believe that this game is mainly for campaign play.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
It's Descent, only quicker. I didn't miss the stuff that got taken out. Seems like there is a lot of mobility and not a lot of conditions that slow down or block players so it feels a bit like a fast break all the time. Still a grinder, just a quicker grinder, if that makes sense. It has grown over me with time. I still think the majority of the 1st Edition's problems were with scenario design (rather than mechanics) but I certainly don't miss original edition tedium like warping back to town in the middle of a dungeon fight for a shopping trip. If I were getting back into Descent (or playing for the first time) this would be the edition-of-choice.

RATING: 5
My attitude has always been, why not just pull out a role playing book, or a better tactical minis game, and do it right? Indeed, that was my feeling about the original Descent, with its slogging night-long gameplay. 

Yet Descent 2.0 just begs to be played with. The dice are wonky, the attribute tests are off-balance, and the minis have so many belts on! So many belts and buckles and little fanny packs! Yet at every dice roll I find myself standing up and cheering in a way I don't for Earth Reborn. And I get invested in the characters as they develop through campaigns, the same way those little monkeys in that experiment got attached to their lifeless, wire-cage mothers.

Also, huge props for a fantasy game I can play with my girlfriend where she doesn't say, "why doesn't that elf put on a shirt?"
---
Update, October 2014: Okay, so if it's not a great game, why do I keep buying expansions for it? 1) Because it's a relatively cheap way to collect and paint plastic miniatures 2) Because I keep thinking I'm going to produce a game my group likes to play out of it. 

Judging by the vast number of attempted variants in the listing for this game, I suspect there's a bad feedback loop happening whereby we keep giving money to a company that doesn't care whether we're having fun or not, in an effort to get them to care about whether we're having fun or not.

And still  there's a sick part of me thinking, maybe they got it right with Imperial Assault? Ugh.

--

See my review for Imperial Assault. In short, my opinion of this game has fallen so precipitously as to have become kind of weird.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
My biggest gaming disappointment for a long time. This is a theme-heavy game without any atmosphere, and a tactical combat game without any interesting decisions.

The tactics are blindingly obvious. Heroes: rush the objectives. Overlord: block the heroes' path by putting monsters at the very obvious choke points. Scenario balance is wildly off to the point where one side cannot conceivably stand a chance in some scenarios; this is particularly an issue in campaign play, where one side's early success can quickly snowball into an unassailable lead. None of this would matter if it was still fun, but it isn't: there's just no game of any note in here.

The components are nice, but even this has a downside: there are far too many superfluous counters. This means that set up time is far, far too long for such a simplistic ("move and roll") game. IMO the nice miniatures undermine the theme, as it seems the designers went for having a wide variety of creatures, but that just  means you end up with unconvincing (silly) combinations of monsters teaming up (justified by an evil overlord controlling them, I suppose, but this contributes to the lack of atmosphere).

It's a shame, as FFG clearly put a lot of thought into the missions and campaign mode, and tried hard to improve and streamline the original. Ultimately, though, it's just horribly broken (and I'm not someone who throws that term around very often).

---
So I've played a full campaign now as the Overlord and the finale was pretty anticlimactic. The heroes (4 played by 2) easily immobilized Splig in the first encounter and pummeled him on their second turn. And then, before the grand battle really even began, my master shadow dragon opened the door where outside the heroes gathered and in 2 firey breaths killed all of them. Dead. Game over. 
Dice games, eh?
---
Conceptually I would rate this game a 10. The components, the heroes, the overlord role, the gear and abilities, the campaign mode, the dice...and oh, the dice. 
In theory, they're an amazing way to randomize attacking and defending.  However, the dice seem to make or break any tactical advantages or disadvantages players gain through clever play. And the scenarios, though varied, seem oddly imbalanced. This might just be a design feature, so the heroes and overlord both get better opportunities to win even when the odds are against them, but it makes some adventures much less fun to play.  I love this game, but only between sessions, thinking about the game, reading some of the class cards, and checking out expansions. A 10 rating when I'm looking at the game, but a 6 or maybe 7 while I'm playing, oddly.  I still can't gel but want all the expansions.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Gorgeous components, very nice Quest and Rules guides, and so very quick to play for a board-based RPG. The play time (after setup) has been 30 minutes to 1 hour tops, which is perfect for my younger players who don't want to get hung up for three or four hours with a standard RPG.

One (big) complaint is that, like others have said, the rules as printed make this a race, and whoever does things faster wins. Another is that the scenario fiction of the game leaves me and my players cold. In fact, my players asked me to rewrite the fiction or they were just going to bag it.

While the rules as written (and scenario setup) are great at times, other times my players and I just want to explore. We've agreed on revised rules that still use the quest setup but totally alter the game goals to allow exploration. I wish the designers had included this style of play in the game.

The figures paint up beautifully.

RATING: 9
Yera

Ademas de las expansiones gordas (y las medianas) tambien tiene adquiridas las expansiones de Lugartenientes (que ni me molesto en poner aqui una por una: son la mini de personaje y un pequeño mazo de cartas para que no te de tanto la impresion de que te estan atracando en toda la cara cobrandote 9 pavos por algo que deberia venir de serie). 

Tambien mencionar las (hasta la fecha, 4) expansiones "cooperativas", que permiten jugar sin "señor supremo", y la app recientemente lanzada (para PC/Steam, iOS y android) que hace ella la funcion de "señor supremo" (todavia no probada)

Es EL dungeon crawler por excelencia en la actualidad. al principio impresiona y nubla un poco al jugador porque hay "muchas cosas que mirar" (sobre todo si juegas directamente al juego expandido). pero al cabo de poco tiempo te mete en la tematica y ya solo quieres matar bichos y cumplir objetivos de la mision. 

jugablemente mejora los defectos mas obvios de la 1º edicion. mejor ajustado en reglas y muy buena escenografia (a pesar del handicap inicial de no incluir ni heroes ni monstruos de la 1º edicion y de la obvia falta de las miniaturas de los lugartenientes). 

Quiza su mayor defecto es que por querer ser muy tematico y al mismo tiempo poco repetitivo a pesar de la repetitividad de sus mecanicas basicas, en ocasiones el juego la lia demasiado con las condiciones especificas de la mision. 

Top 5 y mas alla hasta que otro le destrone en lo suyo, cosa que a dia de hoy veo bastante complicado.

pd. el juego cooperativo da para tocho aparte. valoracion breve: la idea es buena. la ejecucion algo farragosa, pero no regularon bien con la duracion de la partida (o bien yera es un morruo, lo que no es descartable xD)
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I must be an idiot for wanting this, based on my dislike of Descent; but if they can condense something Descent-ish into ~2 satisfying hours, I'm in.
[EDIT] First quest was less than an hour, which was very promising. That said, it was less than satisfying. Cautious optimism leads me to be eager to try the second quest.
[EDIT] So, the double-scenario setup is dubious--it seems at first blush that it likely leads to an inconsequential half (either first or second) too much of the time. And I think they did a great job of shortening, but they took too much out. There's not enough progression in a single session (which is to say there's none), and the underlying combat game is basically too trivial and random to be truly engaging. A yeoman's effort, but just falls short.
