RATING: 7
As a big fan of the original Descent, I had the same mix of excitement and trepidation that many others did. In many ways, 2nd Edition ends up feeling sometimes better and sometimes worse than the original.

So what has changed? Gone are the glyphs, removing travel back to town and shopping in the middle of the adventure. Skills and items are much harder to come by. No longer do potions and money make their way into players' inventories throughout the adventure. Line of sight rules have been changed to a far more lenient (and far less intuitive) system. Armor as a statistic is gone, replaced with armor dice that are rolled. The resulting combats are quicker, and have a feel not unlike the dice rolling in Heroscape, with shields that are rolled cancelling out potential wounds. Adventures are split into two parts, with the first part simply giving each side potential bonuses in part two; this can lead to hoarding of cards and items for the much more important second part. The adventures seem well-crafted (though not always balanced, especially for certain monster groups), and offer a more story-driven experience compared to 1st Edition's more combat-driven one. 

When compared to other adventure games such as the D&D board games, Descent: 2nd Edition is a more strategic and exciting experience. But compared to its parent, much of the epic feel and thematic detail is lost. Ultimately, 2nd Edition succeeds at its goal of streamlining and distilling the gameplay of Descent down to its bare bones. The question still remains whether that was ever an admirable goal to begin with.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
I call Descent 2nd ed my "grown-up Heroquest" as it features upgraded gameplay and miniatures from the 80s nostalgia.  However, I can't rate this above a five.  Ultimately the game is too fiddly with bits strewn all over the place, undersized cards, and no great way to keep track of anything.  On top of this it has some holes in the rules that make you scratch your head.  For a game with top-of-the-line and beautiful miniatures its disappointing it is not more approachable. 

A few items that really stick out:

1. Lieutenants, which are so vital to the game, are represented by flat cardboard tokens while the most mundane of monster gets its own figurine.  This seems like a glaring oversight in a box filled with plastic.

2. The line of sight rules are kind of odd and I recommend creating a comfortable house rule that all players are comfortable with.  Don't forget to the get the errata printed out so it's clear on how board corners work!

3. Diagonal movement on a square-based board.  There is nothing like watching miniatures skip across the entire board in a single turn almost completely negating advantage/disadvantage of speed differences.  This is another item recommended to be house ruled for a more logical experience.

RATING: 6
2013-02-07

Initial Rating: 7.5 (February 2013)

I was skeptical of this re implementation, I really liked the original and I was kind of sad that they decided to include much less miniatures and game material in general, however I found this is a very exciting game and I really want to play the campaign.

I cannot properly compare with the first edition because I only played that game once, however this game is right up to my alley. The theme is perfect for me, the miniatures are gorgeous, the components well crafted, the art is sublime and the gameplay is fast and furious. I was surprised we managed to play a map under 2 hours! 

The double-scenario mission is great, I feel the pace of the game is very fast and I love the 2nd part consequences. The campaign seems to be very interesting with both the heroes and the overlord leveling up. I’m impressed with the map and the “adventures” possible while traveling, very neat idea.  I’m looking forward to play it more (as the overlord of course :devil:).

So far my only complaint is the LOS, I haven’t read the rules, but the heroes were able to target me, even though I was completely behind a wall (for all the good it made them :D), I will have to review this but if it turns out to be correct, I dislike this new simplified LOS.  

This is the right amount of RPG I crave and need, I really like RPG games, but they consume so much time that I had to abandon them, but Descent 2nd edition can fill that hole in a reasonable time frame. 

For now I want to continue the campaign soon and I want to buy it. Also I’m interested in learning all the differences from the previous edition. I’m rating it a tentative 7.5 because this is not a game I would want to play every time, but it is close.

Current Rating: 7.5

RATING: 7
As a dungeoncrawler for more than 2 people, this is the behemoth of the genre. I love to play this (and get to play it a lot, since most of my friends really wanna join the campaign), but sometimes the balance just seem way off. With a smart group of players, the overlord just has very little chance to succeed. For example, just played the interlude where a dwarf hero had to carry a casket to the exit. He had 19 life and 2 healing potions, that could heal him to full with only one action. Thats a lot of soaking, and offcourse I was never close to winning. And that makes me loose interest a little, as I stop caring for what I'm doing as the overlord. I would rather have a fighting chance, and have only won 1 scenario so far (with 2 being really close though, if only I didn't miss with my zombies in 6 out of 7 attacks on an NPC that one time). Really wish it was as nailbiting as Claustrophobia every time
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
I think the game system is pretty good. At least, it inspires me to write custom quests. However, the quests that come in the box are too swingy. Masquerade Ball, for example, is either a cake-walk for the overlord (if they win the first encounter), or an early victory for the heroes (if the overlord has some bad rolls). This could be fixed with house-rules, but as written it's too luck-dependent.

Most of the quests are just a race, with the heroes and Overlord running to their respective win-conditions. There are rarely interesting decisions to make. Each turn is just move and attack, move and attack, with dice determining the outcome. The line-of-sight rules make tactical positioning near impossible.

The story is thin, and role-playing opportunities are sparse. Quests within an Act can be played in any order, so story-line continuity only happens between acts. I tried hard to find the fun in this, but the system fights against fun, seemingly at every opportunity.

NOTES:

* Hero death is insignificant; they spring back up on the next turn
* Monsters can be wiped out before the Overlord even gets to use them
* Quests are unbalanced - it always feels like one side had no chance to win
* Quests are usually a race between the Heroes and the Overlord
* The Overlord's quest goal is often unrelated to the heroes, so both sides tend to avoid each other
* Reinforcements are predictable (usually one per turn) and weak
* Overlord card effects feel weak and insignificant
* Overlord cards are difficult to remember (there are 15 cards in the deck)
* Quest information is public, encouraging players to game the system instead of role-play
* Monsters completely block hero movement
* Line of sight is convoluted
* Figures can miss an attack (1 in 6 chance), which is anticlimactic
* Movement around corners when a monster is present feels unfair
* Large monster movement ("shrinking") feels silly and unfair
* Fatigue-movement feels unfair
* Heroes choose the quest order, so there's no chance for a linear story progression
* There isn't much story to follow anyway - the flavor text for each quest is sparse
* Travel is unrewarding and nearly flavorless

Check out Star Wars: Imperial Assault. It uses the Descent system but improves on it in almost every way.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Completely different game from the first one. I like the shorter play length, the improved balance between overlord and heroes, the class-specific skills. The campaign mode is also interesting and more practical than RtL. The varying objectives of the quest makes playing the OL more interesting.

What I don't like is the removal of the tactical depth of the game: LOS spawning, guard actions, no skill progression during a quest, little/no equipement loot/shopping during a quest, no waypoint, fog of war.

This feels more like a D&D Ravenloft game where the heroes can play it fairly easily. The advantage is that you can combine Descent 1 and 2 together and benefit from 2 systems using the same assets and play whichever game you want depending on the context. Thus this makes Ravenloft, SDE, and other light dungeon crawlers obsolete for Descent owners.

RATING: 7
Ultimately I think that this game is huge missed opportunity. First edition was a great game with several serious flaws which needed to be house ruled. With that done it was an epic game which captured the theme extremely well. 

Second edition brings a lot of good things to that table in principle, but they gutted the heart out of the game. Heroes don't progress during missions and getting better loot is very hard to manage at all. There are not a lot of interesting decisions, either during the scenario or when choosing class cards. Often the scenarios are lopsided, and the first or second half is meaningless (although I will note that when it is tight it works really well).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
I really disliked the original Descent. Too long, too many tiny cards with so many card effects -- I felt that the game was trying to be more thematic by constantly adding rule exceptions. 

Doom, on the other hand, (which used a similar system) I did like. It was unbalanced, but had a nice mix of strategy and luck and positioning, yet the rules remained fairly streamlined.

Descent (2nd ed) goes all the way to the other extreme. Its almost ridiculously simple. Lighter scenarios are a plus, super-streamlined rules and ultimately a game that can be played in an hour. However, I did find myself missing the tactical elements (like placement and overwatch) that the other games in the series had. One really good thing about this game is that the OL can pick monsters for a scenario, really nice touch.

A minor inconvenience for me is the different shapes of all the tiles. There are no consistent shapes. Almost every piece is different. This leads to packing disaster  since you can't group tiles by size and is a real pain when trying to sort out the pieces for a game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

A huge step up from the first edition.  Quest objectives are now more than just "kill the red guy".  Game play has been refined and it now plays very smoothly and it a more efficient amount of time.

RATING: 8
A great system that contains every trope you'd want in a dungeon crawling Ameritrash: dice rolling, treasure, equipment, classes, xp, leveling up, loads of monsters, etc.

There are a couple things that prevent this from being a 9 or a 10. The first is the standard problem with all campaign games: getting the same adults to meet up for the same game. Second is that while every note is touched upon, none of them feel as deeply satisfying as you would like.

With 2nd Edition you are trading the clunkiness and depth of 1st edition for a far more lighter touches and a streamlined experience.

RATING: 5
Personally, I feel something is missing with this 2nd edition.
Yes, the rules are a bit clearer, or at the least, less clouded with minuteau.  Yes, the playtime is easier to swallow, for most (at least those that cared).

But, it's missing that EPIC feel that 1st edition had.

I don't like the character class choices.

I don't like the Overlord's card choices or how his/her deck is built and upgraded.  

I don't like the fact that may/most of the quests are races or puzzles to be figured out.

I don't like that you don't get gear in the midst of a quest, but in between them.

I don't like some of the "rewards" you get if your side wins the 1st part of a quest.

I don't like the base game's finale quest.

I don't like 2nd edition all that much, after all...

RATING: 6
This is a very tactical game, more so that the first edition. It means that there's very little room for errors. All the adventurers have to play with the same set of mind or otherwise they will loose for sure. First edition is more of a looting and shooting type of game. The second edition is more trimmed down version, which means that the looting and shooting is basically gone. All that is left are important tactical decisions that have to be made by all of the players as a team. If one teammate takes a different path or chooses differently than the others then it will probably end bad for the whole team. So overall I like the first edition better since it felt more like an adventure/dungeon-crawler game. This one can also be a fun game but you have to understand from the beginning that this is a tactical team-play game.

RATING: 8

We played through the whole campaign with my son as the DM and my wife and I the adventurers.  I really liked playing this with 3 players as it didn't feel like a lot of downtime. I won't go into it any further for fear of spoilers, but it was a lot of fun and I plan on buying the expansions and am willing to be the DM for my family next time as I think there is enough variety to keep us entertained.

During each game, the heroes embark on quests and venture into dangerous caves, ancient ruins, dark dungeons, and cursed forests to battle monsters, earn riches, and attempt to stop the overlord from carrying out his vile plot.

RATING: 7
Descent is not a bad game, some people might argue that the 2nd edition is actually better or not. From what I know,, it plays faster and that is always a bonus for me.

I only played a quick 45 minute game with low level character.

I was not very impressed because I did not have a chance to do much through the game. I played almost 3 rounds and the game was over. 

Maybe if I would have been a higher level character, I would have had more special power cards and that would have given me more strategic decisions to deal with. 

But that was not the case. So this why I might have found my first game pretty dull.

Now the idea is that it's a campaign game and that you should be allowed to do multiple quest and upgrade your character with time.

Maybe the game gets better by doing so but I don't really have a chance to play a campaign game and I don't have the players which are willing to do so. This is why I could be in the wrong target audience and not be that much interested by the game. I would prefer a game playable out of the box.

Still, for somebody that played hero quest, I think this game is still much better because the game master actually have objectives to win, so there is more competition. But they should have allowed player to play in non-campaign mode like they did for Doom by including death match and other modes like the video game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
The board is small compared to the character speed (1st scenario, 1st level)—smaller than Earth Reborn, Heroscape, Claustrophobia. Nothing wrong with that.

The characters have some obvious "style": I had a brute, T was some kind of healer, etc. Their permanent special power, and a one-shot one tended not to stick in my head, though: I did not fully create a picture of the characters based on their stats and their power. That said, there are not so many heroes, and they have only two powers, so things should get clearer quickly. I felt like Magic fed much more my taste for flavor.

Similarly, when the giant threw away a hero, he ended up rolling back in a corner, in a unphysical way. This felt mechanistic.

I did not like the combats so much: I felt a lack of mission (Heroscape, Earth Reborn, or Claustrophobia give me a stronger sense of a mission to accomplish).

I was also slightly bored when waiting for my opponents. However, turns are fast *and* the party takes actions in any order.

The miniatures are impressive (sizes, variety and number). The number of tokens and cards that we did not use is also interesting. I wonder if the cards won't start feeling like Bloodbowl: Team Manager, somewhat samey between games.

I'm ambivalent about this game: more complex scenarios might be good, knowing the rules better might help, and also playing with an English version (we used a Chinese version).

RATING: 9

Nota:

Una estrategia para el Señor Supremo consiste en una vez que "matas" a un héroe, en siguientes turnos no dejar que se recupere demasiado de vitalidad y volver a matarlo, ya que pierde acciones en reanimarse y además por cada vez que un héroe muere el Señor Supremo coge una carta de su mazo de cartas.

RATING: 7
A well-executed redesign of the original. Descent 2nd ed is a much tighter, smoother-playing design. I especially like that powers doesn't escalate as quickly as in the original, and missions have other objectives than just killing the monsters/heroes.

The balance possibly favours the heroes to some degree. So far the Overlord has yet to win a single mission (after 5 missions with differing overloard/party combinations), but it has been very close on occasion.

Components are very nice and sturdy, and the miniatures are the well-molded standard from FFG. I still prefer the artwork of the original, though.

Play the campaign to experience all advantages of this game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I really like the pseudo level up mechanic in this game and the buying of items. Unlike for example Mice and Mystics you actually feel like you get stronger over time, although a problem is if your side wins more early on you have a higher chance of winning later.

I also like that while there is a large amount of luck in your dice rolls, it is basically impossible to get one shot, and that even if you die you're not out of the game. The way range works with the dice is also pretty nifty since it makes sense you would have a harder time hitting a farther away target, although LoS can sometimes be unintuitive. Also it feels like tactical positioning of your characters to block off routes or to efficiently stay within range is more important than your actual abilities, and turn order of which hero goes first can really cause AP.

Monsters feel significantly different from one another (even sometimes between the red and white versions of each other), and I like how the initial set up makes it feel impossible for the heroes, but the playing field is actually much more level.

Overall gives the most complete dungeon crawl feel of any game I've played as of writing with each encounter feeling reasonably different despite sometimes devolving into hack and slash which prevent it from having as an epic feel as I had hoped.

EDIT: After finishing the campaign, I don't like how damage seems to scale up much faster than health, which makes being one shot before you can do something useful much more likely, although it is cool to see yourself get new abilities (albeit since you get most of them by the end of the game, I don't know if it would be worthwhile to play the same character with the same class again). I would definitely read the errata before each scenario, as there are some things that are pretty unbalanced otherwise. I don't know if it was just my group, but my playthroughs suffered from huge AP and several of the scenarios being extremely easy for the heroes.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 10
I found Descent while I was at my local Barnes & Nobles. It seemed interesting and the reviews I found online had nothing but positive things to say. Fast forward 1 year later and I currently own every expansion and monster/hero pack that has come out so far. Descent is a game filled with so much depth that no matter how many times you play, you'll always find a new path to take to reach victory. There are tons of heroes to choose from as well as monsters to help fight against them as you try and stop the heroes from achieving their goal. While playing Descent i've helped introduce many people into the world of tabletop games who've fell in love with the game play and design. No matter how many times I play or who i'm playing with, Descent is by far my favorite board game of all time.

RATING: 8
A very fun, albeit very casual, dungeon-crawler/action miniatures game. 

The components are very high quality, but there are some serious issues with the dice probability in this game. It looks like no one ran the numbers, as there is in my opinion too much variance in an already high variance mechanic (die rolling). The leap in success rates on Hero Attribute checks (hero attribute of 2 has a success rate of ~22%, and goes up to ~52% with an attribute of 3) is indicative of lazy design in my opinion.

Still a very fun game, with a nice campaign system that is conducive to gaming groups of 3-5 players.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.6
Of all my games this one has changed my opinion the most.  I loved it at first, then went through a long period of hating it.  Now I love it again.  Why?  It just works for my situation.  I have young boys who love playing it and it always brings them to the table.  

The game is also loaded with quality content.  Every piece of the game is beautiful and there are a ton of expansions.  I own the expansions: LotW,LoR,Trollfens, SoN (expansions), CotF, CoD, ToC, and SotS (hero/monster packs) and they all add to the game.  

This is the game I pull out when I'm not in the mood to put together a true D&D adventure and actually fills a nice niche.  It's easy to inject more theme and RP into the game if you use your imagination.  I also love spending time to paint the excellent miniatures.  

I love that this game really feels like a well-executed and polished experience.  It's the Diablo 3 of board games and scratches an itch that other games can't.

What really made this game special to me, however, was the d6 random dungeon generator variant I came up with that turns the game into a true coop dungeon crawl.

Now that the Road to Legend App has been released, this game gets a slight bump in rating.  The fact that you can now run a full coop campaign OR random dungeon (with the Delve add-on) makes this game a far more complete experience and greatly increases the longevity.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I've played a couple of dungeon crawlers ever since I first laid my hands on HeroQuest and IMO Descent 2nd Edition does quite a good job, but could still be improved.

Gameplay:
+ out of the box campaign
+ character development throughout the entire campaign
+ huge variation of monsters and opponents
+ nice custom dice with an intuitive and fun combat system

- character develpment is superficial
- many scenarios seem unbalanced; in our gaming group the OL wins 70% of the scenarios
- it is supposed to be a competitive game, but since it is very unbalanced, it really is not competitive. therefore it would have been better to make the OL more like a game master in the classical sense, who leads the game rather than being an opponent
- Heroes don't die, they simply stand up over and over again; therefore it is worthless trying to kill them at all
- monster respawn rules are stupid, they simply respawn each round; therefore it is often worthless trying to kill them at all
- quests are often merely a race for the exit, which is not fun at all; we want to kill some monsters (or heroes)


Components:
+ top-notch, high quality components
+ a lot of beautiful and detailed miniatures
+ cool custom dice
+ a lot of map tiles and tokens
+ a lot of cool cards with nice artwork

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
As a fan of the original Descent, one has to be careful not to let too much nostalgia creep into the rating of the new version. And, perhaps even more importantly, differentiate between critiques regarding the basic design premise and critiques regarding how well that premise is fulfilled. Dealing with the first issue, it was clear to me before my first play that the design aim was not just to improve on the old game (which certainly could have used cleaning up after every expansion piled on some new mechanics), but a complete rehaul. Whether this is a good move to attract a broader customer base or a missed opportunity to finally put the finishing touches on a nigh-perfect classic is a matter of taste – I fall into the second camp, so a lot of my impression of the new design may be tainted by a very fundamental disappointment.

Regarding the second point of how well this general approach of simplifying the game was implemented, there are a couple of cuts I have absolutely no qualms with: I did not miss counting threat or conquest, and melee/ranged/magic are still nicely differentiated in spite of restricting the system to a single attack die. Together with the great design of the components, it is actually possible to play this smoothly and without referencing the rules, something that only ever happened with the original game when we managed to play once a week and were well acquainted with the rule details.

What I do miss, however, is the range of tactical options in combat. Throwing out offensive fatigue (for surge dice) and the special orders is a severe restriction of how to approach things. Combined with movement/blocking/line-of-sight rules that boil down to Move Everywhere – Shoot Everything, this makes for considerably more bland combat. This shifts the whole game from being tactics-oriented to scenario-oriented, and probably reduces replayability quite a bit. Furthermore, having to rely more on good scenarios to provide lasting interest pushes the game more into RPG territory.

Which, in and by itself, is not a problem, but it makes those rules that are necessary for game balance, but run against narrative sense, more aggravating than in the original game, which made no great efforts to sell its scenarios as anything more than a flimsy excuse to beat stuff up good. Examples we came across in our first game are the fact that anything you don’t search while in the midst of combating the hordes of evil will vanish in thin air as soon as the scenario goal is fulfilled, and the hope to get knocked out instead of just severely hurt between scenario scenes due to the free heal roll. Small grievances, but they get more aggravating when you consider that, instead of inventing new mistakes, the designers could have put their efforts and creativity into solving the mistakes of the original game ...

To end on a positive note, the campaign system is hugely promising, with a clear narrative arc, good theme and interesting decisions for the group of heroes. If I am ever going to write a proper review, it will be after we’ve played through our first go at it, which I am very much looking forward to.

Update: After having played two thirds of a campaign, I'm more and more convinced that reinvention instead of evolution was the wrong path to take for Descent. In spite of the less challening gameplay, there are at least as many instances as in 1st Edition games where our group had to reread, clear up or plain invent rules to keep the game going. Additionally, stuff like the plain horrible rules for large monster movement implodes any sense of immersion that might have come up, and as a pure tactical exercise, Descent 2ed is simply far too simple.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 10
Descent 1.0:
- Long setup time.
- Unnecessarily fiddly.
- Long down time between turns.
- Mindlessly repetitive.
- Incredibly imbalanced (!!!).
- Tedious, boring, monotonous.
- Long take down time.
- "Too much time packed in little fun"
- Might as well play D&D.

Descent 2.0:
- Much shorter setup time.
- Stream-lined rules.
- Very little downtime.
- Not repetitive due to excellent Quest design.
- More balanced.
- Opportunity to plan and strategize.
- In-game timer due to quest and mechanics forces short high-intensity games.
- Still retain RPG elements in between encounters. Isn't it the same as D&D? You level up after encounters?
- Fat juicy theme!
- Flexible game duration.

Updating: 9 to 10. This game is amazing.

This week, Descent 2.0 has officially become my #1 game. Check out my review:

[size=12][b][thread=1013043][/thread][/b][/size]

I've also created a comic-style guide as an aid for new players to get them started on their first game of Descent 2.0 as the heroes. This is meant to be played with 1 experienced player as the Overlord:

[size=12][b][thread=1012610][/thread][/b][/size]

RATING: 9.25
A tense and tight game, that involves a party of heros battling against the evil overlord. A fantastic dungeon crawler, which is a game of tactical miniatures rather than a light adventure.  Important to take every advantage against your opponent, and make every move count (as the overlord can draw more power cards each turn).

Lots of rules and very unforgiving for new players, but definitely worth the investment.  The scenarios vary in length, and given how important each move can be, this game does lend itself to analysis paralysis (which can result in a scenario taking 4+ hours).

Overall it is an excellent game and has a great theme.

Important to play close attention to the victory conditions.  Most scenarios are a race to the finish.  

9>9.5>9.25

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Good: 
The Descent app allows us to play without a GM and handles some of the booking keeping that comes when playing a campaign.  

This game is the dungeon crawl to play when you want to take a party of characters through a campaign, or a series or random dungeons. It is fun selecting a character and building them up in gear and skills to overcome the challenges the app tosses at your party.

This isn't an RPG but it's a nice substitute when you don't have a GM or you have friends over that aren't into RPGs. This is, hand's down, the best co-op dungeon crawl I have found to-date.

Good quality components. 

Setup isn't too bad, the app tells you what to pull out for the scenario, you put your characters on the starting tile, and then you are set to play. 

Scales from 1-4 extremely well.

Bad: 
This game has a lot of components and so it can be a pain to store everything. Additionally, to keep downtime minimal you will likely need to have some sort of organization system to make finding the creatures and tiles easy when the app asks for them. 

Analysis Paralysis when trying to figure out which heroes you would like to play through a campaign with. XD

RATING: 6
This system would have been fine, but I was highly disappointed by the line of sight rules and the fact that it is square- rather than hex-based. Square-based games always make measuring distance and estimating line of sight frustrating because of diagonal "hops." Hex-based systems require more creative level-building, but make the game far more realistic and intuitive. I just could not get over this particular bit of laziness. I have played Descent about five times.

RATING: 5
Makes for a very fun evening, but I suspect it is best if all players lean more towards the storytelling / RPG side of things, and not lean mean min/maxing types who play it as a pure tactical skirmish game. As the former, it has legs, it provides a cool challenge and laughs and a good time for everyone. As the second, it's just a lot worse than many things out there, it's repetititive, simple and without much depth of strategy.

Still, just immerse yourself a tiny-tad and you'll have a great night - slaying monsters, howling over the untimely death of your friends, laughing manically as you manage that last second long-shot that took you from crushing defeat to equally crushing victory. The rules aren't that hard, a bit fiddly if anything but otherwise easy to grasp. The campaign structure of the game is a genius thing - it really wouldn't have any pull with me without it - it's how you can sit and play it for a whole night, or several nights. A single scenario style game wouldn't be able to offer that unless the Overlord put down a whole lot of effort to make a coherent set of scenarios and balanced them.

I like it. I played Warhammer Quest and HeroQuest as a kid, and this is very much in this vein. A good game!

RATING: 10
My original rating was 7ish. I was kind of blah with it after a couple of one-off plays and didn't see why it was rated so highly and everyone was raving. But Descent 2e really began to shine once getting into a full campaign with an overlord and 4 players. Everything seemed just right about it - each encounter was just the right length. With the players and overlord at equal skill levels and rules knowledge almost every encounter has been very closely contested. It is a very satisfying combo of exploration, tactical maneuvering, ingenuity and luck in nice 1-2 hour bite-sized segments. Just recently I've bumped rating to 10 thanks to the hours and hours of fun all 5 of us have had plowing through the SR and LoR campaigns. Excellent game.
2015: Moving rating down to a 9. We have played through 3 campaigns now (shadow rune, Labyrinth and Nerekhall). The game is still fantastic but there are minor issues: lengthy setup, loads of tokens and cards to keep track of, system feels a little clunkier now that Imperial Assault is out.
Mid 2016: Moving rating back to 10 for the excellent Road to Legend app. My Descent group is really loving it.

Me encanta este juego! Yo soy un jugador de dungeon crawlers y este juego tiene todo lo necesario realmente, aunque podría decir que es un adventure crawler.

RATING: 8.5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Descent 2nd Edition is a pretty awful and vastly over hyped reboot. It looks a bit prettier with nicer looking models, but in terms of substance its dumber and shallower, lacking all the charm and charisma of its predecessor.

It might be faster to play, set up and break down again, but its killed all the decision making, fun and depth in the process. I personally never had a problem with Descent 1st being too long. If I actually manage to get a gaming group together then there will usually be a several hour slot for playing a scenario or two anyway! In its defence, Descent 2nd sets up a scenario in about 5-15 minutes and usually plays in about 15-55 minutes.

Its biggest move is from straight dungeon crawling to scenario based missions. It should be more interesting but it actually makes 2nd more prone to balance problems then the original.

It also suffers from the same Hero development overkill problem that 1st Edition does, where by the time the Heroes reach the end of the campaign they are SO strong they can one hit kill almost anything unless they miss. This is even made worse in this version due to huge sets of crazy skill combos making them virtually untouchable, game breaking gods!

As the Overlord they've also rubbed out the dynamic ability to deck build and spend/build threat, leaving you with few effective choices. After a point there is little to spend OL experience on, as only a handful of cards will have a use depending upon your opponents.

I feel overall that those looking for a rich and in depth Dungeon Crawler are best to stick with the original edition.

I plan to take some elements from this version that I actually kind of liked (no special actions, more controlled spawning, no glyphs, recovery rolls) and work them into the old rules, with the aim of clearing up the game play a little (particularly with the aim of making a quicker, playable campaign) but keeping all the juicy complexity that makes Descent 1st what it is.

I thought my stance on this one was around a 6, but after playing through campaigns as both a hero and the overlord and reading the comments I found I'm apparently closer to a 4. I'm going to mark it as such, especially considering all the glory bashing at the other end of the ratings.

Most miserable time playing as an Overlord/Dungeon Master EVER* (*Until I played as Overlord in [i]Imperial Assault[/i] which is the same shit only worse).

This should have been a great evolution, not a poor reinvention.

Also this appears to be the most horrific money grinder ever created- expansions, conversion packs, lieutenant packs, upgraded miniature packs... sometimes FFGs you make me sick.

Progressive Rating: 8-7-6-4.

RATING: 1
This game is horrible. This game had no weapons bonus for the off-hand weapon, there was no buying and selling items, no extra skill cards. There were no feat cards, no upgrading your skills and abilities, and no purchasing training. There were no treasure cards, no gold piles, no points awarded for kills. There was no equipping better gear, no way to get better gear, and no added danger that would require you to do so. There was no danger of losing just from running out of conquest tokens. There are so many things missing, that I could go on and on. The map itself was incredibly small. In Descent first edition, the dungeons are huge with lots of room to move. There was no room to maneuver in the second edition, no way to out flank, no opportunities to achieve your objective in any other way than to go straight at it. This game was not tactical at all. Pretty much you just went towards your objective while the OL tried to stop you, or tried to achieve the OL’s own objective first. I cannot believe this is getting such good reviews. All of those reviews were obviously written by people who have never played the first edition, or who had problems understanding it enough to play it correctly. I, for one, regret that I wasted money on Descent second edition. It was an utter disappointment

RATING: 9

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
The first game I have ever rated as a pure 10. 
So why?
The game production quality is top notch, with all the cards being linen based (even the smaller ones), the board tiles have fantastic artwork and are made of thick board and fit together exceptionally well.
The rules are well written and laid out clearly, and we only needed a few checks in our first game. There is a learning curve but if you have played RPGs or the Ravenloft D&D game it is quick to get going.
The quests are extremely well written and there is a lot more to this than just hacking your way through monster after monster. Both the heroes and the overlord need to work out how to position their pieces and make the most of the character skills.
The First Blood intro encounter works well and leads nicely into The Fat Goblin which is so finely balanced that it came down to the very last dice rolls. Splig was 3 spaces away from escape and victory with his path blocked by 2 heroes and another 2 closing rapidly from behind.... Splig only needed one surge to throw one hero out of his way but failed....with only 7 health remaining, the heroes then took him down....! Who could ask for more.... Even as the losing overlord it was a great end to a near perfect night of dungeon delving! And that is why this game is a 10 in my opinion!

I've now played it with the App as the DM and it works soooo well. Just a really fantastic game!

RATING: N/A
A decent game, the rules take a little working because of FFG's ever present 'oops we forgot to write everything you needed in the book'.

It's all action for the heroes but watching my friend play the overlord he spent a lot of time just sitting around while we decided what to do, how we'd do it and then even executing what we're doing he get's to roll one defence die and that's it.

As a Hero I've enjoyed it but the missions are very random in nature and follow no storyline. My character the warrior seems to be pointless in this edition, killing the bad guy's in most missions is negated by the fact the game is set up to move in a certain way. Either stopping the bad guy's (who move much further than any heroes) from stealing/recovering objectives or trying in vain to stop the bad guy's from quite literally waltzing off the board.

The wizard and healer are immensely weak and the healers healing ability is random and massively under powered. I am hoping that as we progress I can change my opinion of the game.

RATING: 9

RATING: 8
I love Descent as a replacement for traditional pen and paper adventures.  It's a lot easier to get right into the business of running a campaign for those of us without a lot of spare time.

I'm a terrible Overlord, but that makes me a GREAT Overlord, because I am most concerned in making sure everyone is having fun, myself included.  If you just try to crush the heroes, you might end up winning, but you  might also end up with nobody to play Descent with.

My only problem: campaign games just don't work all that well with my group.  Our gaming time is somewhat limited, and these types of games just don't make it to the table all that often.  Might have been fired from my collection by a combination of D&D 5e and Arcadia Quest, but I can't bring myself to part with it just yet.

I also own all of the base-game lieutenant packs, as well as the Shadow of Nerekhall, Lair of the Wyrm, Trollfens, and Manor of Ravens expansions.

EDIT: Love Descent, but my group just can't give it the commitment it needs.  I gave it to a friend who can hopefully play it with his sons!

RATING: 6
Very disappointing game really. I hope to one day play this again (maybe as the overlord this time) but 20 hours is a lot to put into something as patchy as this.  Maybe the app will save it?

RATING: 3
These type of games are just not for me.

I don't like the theme. I don't like the mechanics at all, I don't like silly, boring stories about orcs which invade a lot of games nowadays, and I don't like to have minis in my games. 

I still gave it a chance, since one of my friends bought it recently.

90% of the time, the gameplay felt like randomness that could only be veeeery slightly controlled by decission making. Random enemies spawning and suddenly attacking at me, uncontrolable random stat checks that, of course, I cannot prepare for, random combat outcomes, random events that are not even related to the mission,... I could go on but I think that you see my point.

The other 10% of the game was some silly story about an orc or a Goblin... I can't even remember it. That should give you an idea about how little interest I had in the story that was being told in that particular mission. What I do remember is that enemies keep appearing and dissappearing randomly from every corner of the dungeon. I guess that's a story too.

To make things even worse, the game isn't even easy to play. The rulebook is quite long and there are quite a lot of rule exceptions and small things that you need to be aware of. I don't have any issues with complex rulebooks or heavy games, but the learning effort needs to be rewarded with great gameplay. This game gives me nothing more than dice rolls and bland storytelling.

RATING: 3
One campaign finished (5 players, me Overlord), one midway (2 players, me Heroes).


The campaign left a sour taste in my mouth. I played as the Overlord, and I won most of the games, more often than not [i]with ease[/i]. (Before any one asks: We studied the Quest Guide each session [b]as a group[/b] before playing)

Playing as the Overlord is not very demanding; often [i]I felt like the decisions were very trivial[/i]. Ie. "of course I want the monsters stationed like this to maximize the hinderance of the heroes' movement", "natürlich, that is the way to maximize the number of wounds dealt this round, and has potential for a Total Party Kill", "I should aim to have my entire deck in my hand at the beginning of encounter 2 to really tip the scales in my favor".

One of the heroes became nearly impenetrable tank (Nanok of the Blade, to be exact). This was easy to counter by [i]not hitting him at all[/i], and forcing him to either leave the rest of the party on the ground, or reviving them and not hitting me. At least two of the heroes remained "soft (punching) bags of meat" throughout the campaign.

One player acquired a Rune that had "Surge: Immobalize", and this proved to be one of the [i]biggest threats[/i] to me, since many a time I needed to move fast with a Lietunant.

There's a couple of things that we should've done otherwise to help the heroes:
* At the beginning of the campaign, the heroe's should've built a "team plan": Who's going to play what, and who's going to buy what skills at what moment. (Would this've been as fun as "shopping" your skills after acquiring enough XP?)
* The heroes may have spent too much money in the first act. Really, the price of the items jumps up quite a bit at Act 2, and the monsters grow significanlty at Act 2. This also has to do with the "Team plan": How to spend the money. (No one knew the numbers beforehand)


I will have to try the Epic Play mode, to see this game's mileage as a "single scenario" game.


I thought it would satisfy my dungeon crawl thirst. But [i]this game is no dungeon crawl[/i], it's a tactical level skirmish miniature game, where two teams try to achieve conflicting goals. [b]And that's ok![/b] I can live with that, and it's totally cool. I just find this game a bit [b]too light for what it is[/b].

RATING: 7

 The OVERLORD in second edition feels really, really boring in comparison to how it played in the first edition. The co-op/solo mini-campaigns are very good, and no one has to be the overlord, but they have to be purchased separately.  There are fan made campaign systems which are free, and are quite good.

So for me, Second Edition is better overall, except as the Overlord I don't enjoy it much.

RATING: 7
I've been mostly trying to wipe the memory of the too-long first version from my mind, but the first two scenarios played here are both of an excellent length, 2 hours or so, and brings me mostly back on board. Linking scenarios into a campaign is an attraction, where the degree of success in one helps define what you take into the next. Combat is still a quick single die roll (tick). There's not that many text effects slowing things down (tick). It's not that much different from Ravenloft et al really, except here the Overlord moves the monsters instead of them being programmed. Which makes life harder, but still has probably a touch too much downtime for the adventurers, as well as providing some odd thematic moments (hang on, the zombies moved, attacked, then moved back?? What kind of pussy zombies do that!?). Allowing the players to decide their turn order is the big win, as that gets the conversation going each turn as to how best to proceed, producing a true co-op feel. Combat rolls are weighted towards success but fail at the key moments, causing drama, not a bad thing. So the conversation is going, there's a story being told, the game flows pretty easily. Obviously it's all pretty random as well, but there are decisions to be made. There wasn't quite enough tension to hold me, so maybe I felt the randomness was a tad overwhelming, but I'd be happy to explore the campaign further to see how far the game takes me.

RATING: 7.2
After first try: 

We played the intro scenario and one of the two-part level I scenarios. 

I've played a lot of roleplaying in my youth (mostly AD&D) so I already knew this would appeal to me, but I expected more game and not only "play". 

Even though there was little strategy & tactics involved and mostly move & roll dice I still liked it. You have to see it for what it is. The game has a little more strategy in it and variation than most of the bash-the-zombis that floods the market right now which except for the theme shares a lot with this game, but it's still more an adventure than a game (and more an adventure than the bash-the-zombies games I compare it with).

What makes me like this game appeals to the AD&D-player in me: it's fun to find stuff, buy better weapons and level up. 

My friends have made some modifications to the rules to make the monsters a little weaker and also to make it more strategic with more positioning and moving around insted of static headbanging: monsters have one move and one action each turn. With that the intro scenario was easy but the second part of the next scenario we managed by three very lucky dice rolls in a row with only one health left on the guy we were to save. 

I surely want to get back to this game and continue our campaign.

RATING: 9
I loved this game until I played Star Wars: Imperial Assault (SW:IA), which I believe is a polished version of Descent. Please see my review for that game as well.

* For players who are inexperienced to dungeon crawlers, SW:IA might be a little too intimadating and complicated for them at first. Descent is a tad bit simpler if you want to get newbies interested

* Both games contain a ton pieces to organize and setup, SW:IA has more

* Descent instructions are a little bit easier to read and understand the first time

* Artwork on the Heroe and Monster cards are great and easy to read from the other side of the table and upside down; you can not mix up the health/speed as a heart symbol is behind the health amount and a boot is behind the speed amount. In SW:IA, they are just two numbers that look the same and next to each other.

RATING: 6
Well, on it´s own it´s an ok game, but old Descent is so much better. 

They have simplified it too much and the gameplay has less room for an epic struggle till the end of a quest, much is decided by early mistakes. Levels are more of a puzzle to be solved in exact way than a dynamic adventure of mistakes, successes and close calls. Even dying was more dramatic and you got a fresh start after that.

I miss the power dice, orders, town portals, Hero skill choices - which were more interesting and clever and everybody could train any skill, it was just a matter of who´m it benefits more. I also miss how the doors and chests worked with curses and traps.

The level design is not so good either, the objectives start to repeat themselves if you play the whole campaign. Some levels are broken (unwinnable by one side) and some just plain annoying - the one where both sides have to get their stone from the other side and drag it out of their side took forever to play through.

RATING: 9
Excellent campaign system. Great co-op vs. overlord experience. Seems well balanced so far. Plays smoothly and quickly while retaining depth of tactics and strategy. Plenty of thematic flavor and fun scenarios. 

While there are a lot of options available to the player which increase as they progress, the game remains fast for what it is. I was shocked that we got through the the first two scenarios of the campaign in just a little over two hours. It sure didn't feel like it though, seemed more epic than that. Very satisfying. Great narrative comes out of this game. 

A whole lot of epic in such a short play time is very impressive. The whole group loved it even more than they thought they would. This version of Descent is going to get played a lot on game night, oh yes indeed!

Another bonus is the game is actually transportable now. Can easily fit into a backpack...that is until the expansions are released.

Edit: ok initial shine has worn off. The game seemed great in the beginning but the flaws are showing up now. Balance seems swingy, especially with the conversion kit heroes and monsters. Insufficient play testing is painfully obvious in some cases. 

If the hero team is set up and planned out well the overlord doesn't have a chance. Players not sufficiently familiar with the games typical tactics requirements will be destroyed by a knowledgeable overlord at first.

Some encounters favor one side or the other. Certain monster combinations are difficult for the heroes to deal with in some encounters.

sometimes a hero can find themselves in a situation where they are being knocked down and revived again repeatedly for the entire encounter making for an unhappy player.

despite these issues the game is still enjoyable. It's just not as awesome as previously thought.

Edit: expansions helped deal with some of the issues this game had. The new Road to Legend app breaths new life into this game and moves the rating up.

RATING: N/A
Not remotely as good as the original IMO (the loss of the "monster placement outside of LOS" rules, the town-portals, and the Overlord's point generation based on number of heroes in dungeon, robbed the game of most of its positioning-based tactical depth).  Too many of the 2e campaign missions can be cheesed via simplistic choke point strategies.  Some Overlord missions are trivially easy to win if you just plug a hallway with a fat Ettin or Dragon for long enough.  I love the art and design of the tiles and cards, and I love the "wilderness travel" section and the overall design of the campaign mode, but 2e overall just doesn't thrill the way the original did.  I'm working on developing my own hybrid rules that will allow me to use 1e rules with 2e's gorgeous tiles and boards and campaign module.

RATING: 9
OWN...
 - 2nd Edition Base Game
 - All Small + Large Boxed Campaign Expansions
 - All Print-On-Demand Co-Op Expansions
 - All "Hero & Monster Collections"
 - All "Lieutenant Packs"
 - Extra Dice Pack (Power Dice: 2xRed/2xYellow/1xBlue,  Defence Dice: 2xGrey/1xBlack/1xBrown)

----------------------------------------------------
Exactly our type of game, we love it!
But we had various concerns before purchasing...
>>[Our updated notes after playing are in square brackets, for each point]
-----------
- Very long playtime, especially for campaigns
>>[Each quest actually plays plenty quick enough for us, and we can decide whether to do individual quests, or full campaigns. It's easy to separately bag each player's stuff between quests, to resume later. Mini-campaigns in some expansions help here too!]

- Way too many expansions, some of which may be essential, since base game quest/campaign is supposedly not the greatest or most balanced (although the reasonably-priced "Heirs of Blood" campaign book only requires the base game, so is a very good buy)
>>[Well, we liked it enough to buy it all anyway! And I've seen plenty of reports of other people doing similar!]

- Errors/ambiguities in the rules? which still haven't been fixed in current stock?
>>[Most errata have actually been applied to the version of the base game we have, I believe just one card & one place in the rules still needed correcting as of FAQ v1.5. But the expansions were mostly un-erata'd.]

- It's an evolution of the Doom board game, but that didn't really gel with us for some reason (don't entirely remember, but maybe it seemed hard work & fiddly at the time as a 2P, 1 person controlling multiple characters, 1 person having to play the bad guys - Space Hulk was a much simpler proposition along similar lines) 
>>[Maybe it's the fantasy setting, or the more varied abilities etc, but this game has just pulled us in more than Doom The Boardgame ever did. And so far, we've had no issue controlling & keeping track of multiple heroes when playing 2P.] 

- Maybe there'll be a 3rd Edition at some point, which incorporates changes/improvements from Imperial Assault (a more recent Star Wars themed variation of the same game).
>>[I daresay it's highly likely there will be a 3rd Edition, at some point... but that may be a number of years away. And I can only hope there'll be a relatively easy/inexpensive upgrade path, although unlikely. I would be hesitant to get something like the upgrade kit from Descent 1e => 2e, as there were many fixes required to various monsters/heroes as they have been properly re-released in 2e Hero & Monster collections. We have plenty of game with all the 2e expansions that we'll probably never need to bother with a 3rd Edition anyway!]

- We already own Gears of War + Space Hulk which are better suited to our available gaming time/sessions, yet still have elements of the tactical battles featured in Descent. And we also own Runebound + many other questing games which incorporate the fantasy elements. We just don't have anything which marries the two.
>>[Am glad we opted to get Descent, as it is plenty different enough to anything else we own, and our concerns over play times were unfounded. It's easy to break a campaign into shorter sessions.]

- Hoping that "Warhammer Quest: The Adventure Card Game" manages to incorporate enough of the elements we'd like from Descent (character/ability development, combat, multi-quest campaign), though presumably that would lack the tactical movement etc. And WHQ:TACG also seems to have a similar action system to Space Hulk Death Angel, which we also like. It is also likely to be much cheaper, even if multiple expansions eventually release.
>>[We also now have WHQ:TACG" anyway - more choice to suit our mood!]

RATING: 7
Better the the first edition in many ways.  I'v played both sides a few times now.  The scenarios seam a but silly and there is so much variability in the dice that is can be irritating. For example I did really well on part 1 of the goblin quest(as overlord) but i missed so many torture rolls on part 2 that the bonus I got for winning the first part was irrelevant.  (I got the correct guy with 3 archers left to me, granted we messed up the doors and my spiders got AoE'd but I was still a good 6 rounds from the exist just sprinting not to mention getting hammered on by the hero's the entire way... this for winning the first encounter.)

I inevitably have to compare this Earth Reborn, witch seams a lot less asinine in many ways.  In that game you can loose a character in one hit due to bad dice rolls.  Further more the movement and line of sight mechanics are a hell of a lot less retarded, yes they may leave no room for questions, but they are just stupid, you cant take cover, retreat, or even get out of line of sight because only a corner has to touch and diagonals count. Also taking damage/dyeing in that game actually matters where as in decent you simply lose 1 action and the overload gets one more card, witch is just another card to do a bit more damage and drag the game on a bit longer.  If for example when you died it advanced the plot like in the first addition then players would actually about dying.  As it is it is just a "grab all the treasure" exercise.

RATING: 6
[b]Comment:[/b]  It's a fun pseudo-RPG/Adventure game, where players compete against the GM player to try to complete a scenario goal first. This game has a lot of cool looking minis, and I enjoy the way players increase in skills and powers as they invest experience points into their various skill trees.

Frequently base game quests are hard to understand the rules to, leading to game play effecting mistakes being made. This has improved over time, in particular the [thing=137842][/thing] quests seemed very well thought out. 

Frustrating that there are minis included for characters, for monster groups, but not for bosses. Boss monster minis are sold separately. 

I also wish the heroes could invest points into multiple professions. There's so much content in this game, it feels a little overwhelming that you can play the same class and have a near infinite character/skilltree combinations to pick from.

There is the [thing=104163][/thing] which gives you tons of new toys to use with this game. The results are mixed in terms of balance, and this has now become of questionable value as they are re-releasing these guys in small numbers through separate expansion packs.

:star: Art and components
:star: Innovative mechanics
:star: Handles 5 or more players
:nostar: Good with min or max players
:star: Low time between turns
:nostar: Comfortable complexity
:star: Re-playability
:star::nostar::nostar: Enjoyment

[b]Critical expansions:[/b]

[thing=137842][/thing]

[b]Good expansions:[/b]

[thing=129423][/thing]
[thing=143827][/thing]

RATING: 8
Fun and engaging even with its setup/clean-up time, and the occasional drag of checking forums for doubts. Lots of colorful cards and bits, and lots more available through the pile of expansions Fantasy Flight puts out for it to keep everyone giddy. If you like the game you'll probably end up picking some. If you don't think the overlord player wont be overwhelmed by a little bit of extra rules, I recommend getting one Lieutenant Pack, as the included plot decks add a nice layer for decision making. There are 16 available for now, and I'd recommend one of the following: Bol'Goreth, Merick Farrow, Ariad, Tristayne Olliven, Splig, Verminous.

A game to play with fellow board game or video game geeks, and not with the family on Thanksgiving. It requires a steady group to play the campaign mode, which utilizes the fun part of XP and purchasing weapons with gold. Look for the How to Play video series on YTube, it helps.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Each edition of Descent seems to want to recreate a role-playing game on a board, which I do not think it does well.  Having played through roughly 10 hours of this edition, it is definitely more of a tactical experience, and less of a role-playing experience.  I really wish it wouldn't bill itself as an adventure game, as there is little to no exploration, one must compact their turn into precise moves bent on achieving a certain objective, anything short of this typically ends with failure on the part of the "adventurers."

As a tactical experience, I give this game a high rating, but it combines with a low rating at failing to follow in the wake of the great adventure/role-playing board games such as Magic Realm, Hero/Warhammer Quest, and Buffy the Vampire Slayer.

The lack of a Sauron-like ruling villain also makes the Overlord role a bit disjointed as this single player takes on the random groups of antagonists time and again.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
This is a great campaign-based game. In my limited experience the quests seem pretty well balanced as long as the heroes play very efficiently - there is definitely an edge in favor of the overlord.  I believe this game rests on the shoulders of the overlord for this reason.  As the overlord I play with the hope that the heroes win, but I do my best to make sure it comes down to the last turn.

Some overlords will probably play very competitively and I believe it's possible for the overlord to demoralize the heroes too much.  The fun of this game rests in the overlord's ability to balance the game towards fun.

Recently I've found that the game seems so overwhelmingly in favor of the overlord (me) and I simply can't do anything to give the heroes a chance.  We are still enjoying the game, but I think the campaign mode of this game may have a problem with a "run away leader" problem for the overlord.  I won act I and moving into act II seems impossible for the heroes right now.

I literally have half of my monsters just running in circles and still destroy the heroes.  The heroes playing the campaign with me are made up with several casual gamers so I don't expect them to min-max their heroes.  Because of this I feel obligated, as the overlord, to adjust the difficulty to account for this.  At this point, however, I almost can't lose unless I just have my monsters open all the doors and just run into the corner.

RATING: 6
Overall, this is probably the best of a large number of conventional fantasy dungeon-crawling games.

A lot of its modern streamlining is appreciated (especially the clever, quick combat dice system) - but it is still trapped within that tired 1980s-style 'move and hit things with your axe' format. So it's quite shallow stuff.

I'm always amazed at how long each turn takes. I never feel like I've done much after 2 hours of play. The decisions that you have to make are relatively routine but still take a while to discuss - a bit like choosing what colour to paint your living room - so I do notice some players getting bored/distracted until its their turn.

You also get bogged-down in having to check rules on cards etc quite regularly, although that's much quicker than in games like Merchants & Marauders, Dead of Winter, Twilight Imperium or Eldritch Horror (all of which I think are too sluggish and fiddly to deserve their high ratings).

(As an aside - I've played Descent's reimagined incarnation, Imperial Assault, once. That seemed minimally different to Descent 2 and I think I might actually prefer Descent 2's way of handling player turn order ... although some Imperial Assault tweaks seemed useful.)

RATING: 8
Descent 2 is not exactly a dungeon crawler.
It rather is a tactical fantasy game, set within an "dungeon crawler" environement.
About all map and monsters data are known from the beginning.
But the game is lighter than the first edition, faster and really fun to play.
One can easily introduce newbies to the system.
Of course, some people have complaints about some simplified game abstractions and about the fact that killing monsters and hoarding treasure is not the first aim of the quests - which offer diversified and, let's say it, much more intelligent objectives.
The mix between tactics and fantasy theme (the game is immersive) is what I really had been waiting for quite a long time.
This is my best game acquisition in 2012 !

RATING: 6
Would prefer that this game involve more tactical considerations aside from what ability to use and when.

As it is, facing and positioning matter very little. Regardless of which direction it is pointing, a character can attack any other model within its range. Nor are characters granted penalties/bonuses for their orientation relative to other models. So an Overlord's best strategy seems to be clogging choke points, such as turning 2x1 models sideways to block hallways.

The game would benefit greatly with the addition of backstabs and flanking.

Though I've only played once, so take my observations with a grain of salt.

RATING: 9
14-04-2014. I do like Descent 2.0. I currently own all lieutenants for the basegame, the basegame itself and the first expansion. I also pre-ordered the coop kit, since it seems extremely cool.

The biggest downside of Descent 2.0 compared to 1.0, is the streamlining. Yes, they stream-lined much and much is indeed for the better! It plays quickly, the scenario's are really different. The artwork of Descent 2.0 is gorgeous, the mini's way better than 1.0. They've done away with random skills. The overlord lost his ability to spawn new creatures..

Problem I have with 2.0 compared to 1.0, is that the games overall feel WAY less epic, but more like a puzzle. Descent 1.0 is epic, long and involved. Because the OL can spawn, you cannot figure it out. Yes, it's difficult to get a group for 1.0. But if you have the group for it, I'd much rather play 1.0 than 2.0. 

01-10-2013. I love this stuff. I like playing overlord, killing my opponents.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
GOOD game. Definitely an improvement over the expansion-heavy ruleset of 1e. The Quests are also much more imaginative. If you like 1e, there's no need to change. But if you missed 1e or couldn't get it to the table, go 2e!

*****

It's *really* replayable.

The OL gets to choose which monsters to play in a quest, and each monster group has its strengths and weaknesses. These strengths and weaknesses aer dependent on the quest and the player's heroes. Furthermore, the OL can customize his deck differently. So, from a tactical miniatures viewpoint, the OL should have enough variety. Likewise, the players have eight classes to choose from, and will choose different skills within a class.

You can also play D2E as a conventional roleplaying. D2E now has Willpower, Might, Knowledge, and Perception skill checks, which are skills often used in fantasy RPGs. After playing the quests, an OL should have enough familiarity to design his own adventures!

This is a very cool one-vs.-many experience, meant to be played over a long campaign of multiple sessions (which can be an issue if you aren't getting together and playing it regularly).  It has great components, from the map tiles to the awesome miniatures, and the cards and character development are pretty neat.  I think that the Heroes' character development is not as interesting as the Overlord's, and so too the gameplay, but this can be resolved by having the Hero players each control multiple characters.  I also feel that there are some balance issues depending on player count.

Overall, I am glad to own the game, but don't see that it will get a huge amount of play, and sadly I am not always enthusiastic about playing it if I am not the Overlord and/or the player count is not optimal.

RATING: 6.75
Much simplified from 1st edition.  Still hard to get on the table unless you have the right group (I don't).

My big complaint on this one is 2-fold:
1. It feels much more like a combination of a tactical combat game and a race game than a dungeon crawl. How can I (as the overlord) position my dudes to slow them down?  Not because of some theme, but because I want a benefit in round two...meh.
2. For as simple as FFG made this edition, it still has a lot of fiddly stuff going on.  I tried to make a cheat sheet for my friends who didn't play as often as I do and it was still a piece of paper front and back.  I guess if you play an entire campaign with the same group, that isn't an issue.

I'd still play a campaign if someone offered, but it wasn't something I needed to own myself.  I think I'd have proffered the based game as a pure co-op.  For asymmetric combat, I'll play something else instead.

RATING: 7
I own D1e but still haven't played it - traded it a while ago but had limited interest off other players to try it. Playing 2e has rekindled my interest in trying out 1st. 

Things I like - the sculpts are vastly superior on the minis and the scales seem in better proportion - too many ginormous models in 1e for my taste. Art again is a vast improvement - the character art in particular. Also, general aesthetics, layout, etc are all several notches up. And minimal need to refer to rules is commnedable. Play itself: So as stated, I haven't experienced the notorious 5 hour drudgery of 1e's kill cycle, although I suspect I would get a kick out of it. 

The colour to the scenarios is a lot of fun, but I wonder if they have much replayability. Most are over very quickly, and tend to have odds stacked to one side or the other, with this being offset by only minimal impact of winning or losing. Bizarrely the most significant encounters can be the random road encounters (my crew took a bigger haul of items from one of these cards than across two scenarios). But I do like the pace of progression - which is to say tantalisingly slow. It's the progression experience of an RPG campaign condensed across a couple of weeks rather than a couple of years or (in D1e's case) a couple of hours. Playing the OL seems inherently less interesting, with fewer choices and decisions than 1e gave. 

On the whole, 2e seems to be a really good intro to 1e - but until I actually experience 1e for myself I should avoid drawing conclusions from my experience so far. Enjoyed it a lot, but it leaves me wanting more. Will my 1st edition plus the cards from the expansions (thanks to the secondary market caused by 2e on 1e cannibalization) and Road to Legend satisfy that craving? Time will tell...

RATING: 8.9
I may be biased considering how much money I spent on this game, but in general I think it's an extremely enjoyable dungeon-crawler. Since the app, Road to Legend, has been released this game can be enjoyed as a competitive as well as fully cooperative experience.

Furthermore there is a large community behind the game and by now an open source application called Valkyrie has come up, which basically works similar to the official coop-app, but also allows allows players to design their own fully cooperative scenarios and campaigns and share them with the community.

Between the normal one-versus-many campaigns of the base game and various expansions, the print-on-demand non-digital coop expansions, the official app, the official quest vault for user-made one-versus-many campaigns and scenarios and now the open source app: Descent offers such an excessive amount of replayability and content, that basically every single other dungeon crawler on the market pales in comparison.

RATING: 8

The rules are very straightforward for a game with this much depth- of particular note for me are the "line of sight" rules, which in a game like this could easily threaten to derail a session with nitpicking and hair-pulling. RATING: 8
+ Base set
+ Shadow of Nerekhall
+ Manor of Ravens
+ Forgotten Souls
+ Nature's Ire
+ Heirs of Blood
+ Skarn
+ Crusade of the Forgotten

Was looking for a dungeon crawl game and picked this up. Completed the Shadow Rune campaign, and now halfway through Manor of Ravens. Have also played single quests, co-op with the forgotten souls expansion as well as the Descent RPG variant and using the Road to Legend app. 

Have painted the base set minis

* Looove the tiles in the game. Lots of variations included
* Nice minis in the game. I just look at pics of the painted minis on BGG and drool.
* Different heroes with different classes play differently, so there is a lot of variation included in the box.
* The ruleset is simple and easy to follow.
* Some people complain about the game balance. While that is true for some quests, we mostly found it balanced. In our Shadow Rune campaign, the heroes won 5 quests (including Interlude & Finale) while the overlord won 3 quests.
* This is a purely tactical strategic game, there isn't much narrative involved
* The Road to Legend app is awesome!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I personally love it when games are close in the end. Unfortunately, this was never the case with Descent in any of my plays. A quest always ended with one side totally crushing the other side. Nearly all of the Act I quest are horribly unbalanced, e.g. Cardinal's Plight (Overlord), Castle Daerion (Overlord) and Death on the Wing (heroes).

The problem I see with this game is that there is too much randomness in it that never evens out during a quest because they are so short. The randomness comes from:
- The dice rolled for attacks
- The dice rolled for defense (this is the biggest problem regarding instant kills)
- The dice rolled for attribute tests
- The cards drawn by the Overlord
- The random elements in the quests (sometimes with additional attribute tests)

The biggest problem I have with this game:
The gameplay is not designed around fun but frustration. This includes the frequent attribute tests, where the Overlord is encouraged to pick on the same heroes over and over again. Also Overlord classes like Saboteur or Punisher penalize the heroes for achieving something or interrupt their actions. The campaign gets increasingly difficult for the side that loses often, making it pointless and frustrating to continue. Movement is very important for most of the scenarios and quest objectives, of course the Overlord is able to annoyingly hinder your movement in a variety of ways, leaving you standing around doing nothing. Also picking heroes with the right combination of attributes feels too important. Thus, if you play with a group that doesn't enjoy direct conflict in the meanest of ways, then they will pretty much hate this game.

And another problem:
The first quest (after the Introduction) will often decide the outcome of the whole campaign:
If the heroes win, they will pick their next quests from The Fat Goblin, Death on the Wing, and Masquerade Ball, most certainly winning all of these.
If the Overlord wins, he will pick Castle Daerion and Cardinal's Plight, winning both and with 3 wins under his belt, he will continue to win.

To summarize: FFG have once again shown that they are not capable of designing a balanced scenario-based game (other examples include 1st Edition Descent, Battles of Westeros, Mansions of Madness, Doom (2004), DOOM (2016), Tide of Iron, Tannhäuser, etc.).

RATING: 7
I expect rating to go up when I play this as a campaign since that is how you will use most of the games content. Might give epic play a try as an alternative, but I didn't really like that mode of play in Mutant Chronicles: Siege of the Citadel, so I suspect campaign will be the way to go just like it was there.

Problem with basic one-shot scenario play is that without more abilities in play (as a reuslt of buying equipment or leveling up) the tactics are a bit simplistic. I haven't seen any potential for truly clever tactical play so far.

The heroes seem squishier than usual, which is fun for Overlord (attacking invincible looted up heroes can be frustrating) and in return the penalty for death is very low for the genre which takes away some excitement I think. Most missions for the Overlord seem to be based on "complete this objective by getting X and escaping", rather than just killing the heroes, slowing them down is the objective.

Rating with Basic Play No Campaign: (4 Design + 3 Enjoyment)

RATING: 9.5

  Solo or co-op scenarios are now available
 I am fortunate that I play with someone who is a keen overlord player (and good) and he is also willing to get involved in the story and theme which greatly adds to the enjoyment of this game.
  I usually pick semi-random or themeatic heroes ,if you were to pick purely the best then I imagine it could get stale. I have now played all the campaigns and only used 3 heroes more than once (I usually play 4 heroes).
  I feel that I must update my comment since the release of the Road to Legend app.  Although I am firmly against anything computerised in my board gaming this app proves there are exceptions, it greatly adds to what was already a great game. They seem to have got the AI right as you will lose if you don't play well even on normal setting. Even our overlord admits that it plays better than him and now loves being a hero and can't get enough of the treasure which he previously hated. Surprise encounters, buying items from town, dangerous perils, well set out adventures and a decent story have made this one of my very few 9.5's.  If you enjoy Dungeon Crawlers with a well thought out campaign nothing else comes close at the moment.
