=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I was a little let down by this game.  That's not to say it's not a good game, I enjoyed myself and look forward to playing again (we have a weekly group set up to run through a campaign).  I'm just saying I had lofty expectations of being blown away by it and I simply wasn't.

Part of it might be because my first (finally!) play of the game was just the introductory encounter.  That said though, I've been playing games long enough that I can usually decipher a lot of what I think about a game from a first play, even if it is a "learning game".

Descent is a very simplified and streamlined dungeon crawl.  A scenario is set up, each player plays a hero against an overlord player who manages all the monsters.  Each side has a win condition and heroes have abilities and equipment they can use to their advantage, as do the monsters plus the overlord has a hand of cards that allow him to use special abilities.  All of this fits into the usual fantasy tropes.

Where I was let down was I felt it was a bit TOO simplified and streamlined for my tastes.  Each hero starts with only 1 or 2 abilities and pieces of equipment and when he levels up the choices are pretty scant.  The maps are a bit claustrophobic and the scenarios (I've read through a few, which isn't cheating by the way, there's always 100% open information) are a bit too simple in their goals and how they're laid out.  I'm also a bit worried that it might suffer in the same way Mansions of Madness does in that while this has more content than Mansions it will still run out and playing through the same capaign repeatedly loses some of its flair.

I guess I'm just spoiled by my tabletop and digital RPG experiences but I just wanted more.  That said it still delivers quite a bit of fun and strategic gameplay and dilutes it down to 2 hours instead of 6.  And watching your character grow delivers a little bit of the same rewarding feeling you would get in those other RPGs.

I'm now currently playing as the Overlord and it is a little less fun because I have yet to win (with my other group, as the heroes, we always won as well).  I'm convinced that this game is not very well balanced for the heroes vs. the Overlord and it has a bad rich get richer problem.  Once heroes in this game realize how powerful the items make them they will strive to get all the treasure counters in each scenario and continue to get stronger while the little bit of advancement the Overlord gets in the form of one time use cards they may or may not see does not even out this experience.

Some of the expansions include a little bit more powerful monsters but it is too little, the game is very one sided.  In the end though the amount of content and the game play keeps bringing me back.  I also enjoy the challenge enough and I've come close to winning a couple times so the motivation and hope that I will win at least one quest is still there.  It's just a much more fun game as the heroes and if this sounds incredibly frustrating to you, you should always stay away from the Overlord role.

I FINALLY won one quest, the second half of Death on the Wing.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Dear Fantasy Flight,

you are forgiven for Gears of War, Mansions of Madness, RuneAge, Dust Tactics, lord of the rings and every other pile of chromed up, pedestrian playing, lacking in imagination, poorly playtested, piece of donkey do from the last three years because this game is genius.

The system is so stripped down from a rules read through it looks shallow, but game play is just so tense and involving unimpeded by 10,000 chits or Terrinoth waffle (its there but the table looks less like a town centre pavement after pub closing and more like a mission). For once the design hits the spot, game first, chrome second. This is the grail Dungeon crawl - the rules are simple, 15 minutes setup per encounter and and an hour or so play per scenario. Plus there is a campaign, with incremental development for both Players and Overlord. At no point does the game stall and at the trackable information is at just the right level - its possible to keep it all in view and mind yet theres enough going to provide depth to the story and meaningful choices for all participants. A huge improvement on Descent - which was damn fun to begin with. Where there was grind there is now flow, where every encounter went on longer than a Frankie Howard death scene now there is one decisive, bloody and brief tipping point...

RATING: 6
Overall, a nice game because of the built-in campaign.
Otherwise it is a watered-down and rather boring version of first edition.

In a few areas the game has improved, adding innovations such as character classes and skill trees but overall it gives players less choices and seems oversimplified without an epic feeling. 
Either you win or lose an encounter, the difference (reward-wise) is tiny. No matter how badly you get slaughtered by the overlord, you both just gain 1xp and he gains a minor bonus and vice versa.

I would always play the 1st edition campaign (Road to Legend) if i had the choice. It feels much more gamey, requires more involvement and planning in character development and is not as forgiving if you fail an encounter.

+ Skill trees/Character classes.
+ Monster group activation.
+ Encounters have alternative ways of winning rather than simply kill em' all and escape.
+ Built-in campaign.

- Not so epic as 1st edition.
- Many rules are oversimplified and leave players with few choices.(No threat,no hero respawn,no city actions,no guaranteed potions etc.)
- Few combat dice

RATING: 7
Rating after 6 plays.
First the bad news. Raw have the worst LOS rules ever. Easy to work out but defying all logic. 
Had to switch to point to point LOS to enjoy game. Far more intuitive and just as easy to work out IMHO. 

Now the good news. I love everything else about this game. The Campaign mode is made up of 9 quests, most of which are quick two part encounters that can be played and recorded separately. The second of each of these encounters offers an additinal relic item to reward your future quests. The first encounters offer no reward but does determine the  difficulty of the prize-giving second encounter for the heroes. If the heroes do badly in the first encounter it will be very difficult for them to win the second. 

There are tech tree abilities to be developed for both the heroes and the Overlord. Equally interesting choices for both.  

Great components, and well designed quests that encourage a race through the dungeon or map rather than a drawn out slugfest. 

Rating could go up to an 8 or 9 after more plays if the quests are balanced and varied enough.

House rules:
Heroes:
Ancestor Spirits (Spirit Speaker) - does not exhaust to use.
Stoneskin (Spirit Speaker) - does not exhaust to use.
Runic Sorcery (Rune Master) - exhaust to use.
Scenarios:
First Blood - (A) Heroes win if Mauler is destroyed AND no search tokens are left on the board. (B) Mauler can destroy an adjacent search token for 1 action.
Cardinal's Plight (Enc 1) - if there are no zombies or graves, the encounter ends without counting as a victory for either side.
Cardinal's Plight (Enc 2) - activating the alter removes all status conditions from the Cardinal.
Dawnblade (Enc 1) - the sunlight moves at the end of the hero turn.

RATING: 8.5
With the right players this game is great fun.  Some of the scenarios are very video game like but they work for what it is. The set up for these scenarios might not be worth it but I can live with that for some dungeon slashing goodness.

update : Having played some scenarios twice now I see how good tactical maneuvering can really change each one.  Very cool.  It's a simple system in favor of easy moves by the heroes but it works well to keep the fun factor high.  Dice rolls are stand up tense in some situations.  I hope with more plays (in the campaign) it will get a better sense of leveling up for the heroes.

update: Now, halfway through the campaign this game has really become a great time.  We usually have at least 4/5 players and are really getting our characters development going on.  We switch out the overlord role seamlessly.  I'm excited to see the strength of the part two monsters.  Everyone is getting expansions so we will have a nice crop of monsters to contend with.

update: Finally played through an entire campaign.  It really can get tough in the second act.  Some quests are pretty easy due to the massively powerful heroes at this point.  Great game that I am willing to muster up the energy to play it for extended periods of time.  

Expansions: 
Oath of the Outcast
Forgotten Souls

RATING: 8
Wonderful D&D-style board game with a lot of really deep RPG mechanics.

The only thing that makes me sad is that Descent is best played as a Campaign game, where you play through all the quests in the campaign in order. You *can* play one-off games--just choose a quest, make some characters, and throw them in there--but it's not quite as much fun.

It's also a fairly unforgiving game. This is *NOT* a leisurely dungeon crawl. Every quest has an objective, and you have to run after it as fast and hard as you can or you'll get crushed.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Much "quicker" system than Descent 1st ed. There is a lot to like over the original: built in campaign, simplified LOS, shorter scenarios, much better quality models. 

The game itself is much faster. You can still play a large number of quests if you want a lot of game. If you are strapped for time, you can play a single encounter fairly quickly.

**Update June 2016
FFG came out with an app that automates the overlord (much like the standalone co-op adventures), but campaign style. So now this can be a true solo or co-op game. Unfortunately you cannot play the content that comes with the game and its expansions, you only play the content in the app. Thankfully, there are a couple of really good manual automated systems out there.

This is a great dungeon crawler whether you play the overlord vs players (though that isn't always balanced) or one of the many options for solo/co-op play. There is a wealth of expansions to increase the different heroes, archetypes, monsters, etc. This is the third generation of this system (Doom, Descent 1st ed, this) and the system grew again with Imperial Assault - in fact, a couple of the solo systems use a similar turn order as IA. At any rate, it is a lot of fun. FFG has done a great job in making Campaigns (though the original base set campaign is not very good, the others are good and the base set was replaced by Heirs of Blood in other editions - for those with the original, you can purchase the hardbound HoB campaign book.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
So much fun. Each person is a different class with different abilities; there is a campaign that consists of several quests; you can level up your character to gain new abilities; you can find treasure during missions; you can buy and sell equipment; each scenario has different objectives. Just great.

My rating is also based on the system as a whole, not just the base game. The base game is okay, but when you add some of the expansions you have a ton of variety in heroes, monsters, items, quests, and classes.

[b]Road to Legend update:[/b] After many, many games of Descent, I have to say that the one-vs-all nature of the game is its biggest drawback. It tends to create frustration: either the overlord is getting crushed and has a table full of other players who are all celebrating his defeat, or the overlord gets the advantage and gleefully attacks the disgruntled players.

Road to Legend turning the game into a pure co-op is brilliant. It works great and is very similar to Mansions of Madness 2.0, where the app just tells you how to set everything up and it keeps up with a few things, but it doesn't get in the way or take over the game.

The app keeping up with heroes' gold and xp is takes away all of the bookkeeping that you had to do previously. The town section is great because it gives you some real choices between quests now: take time to refresh the shop to hope for better items, go on some sidequests, or do special activities to gain more gold or xp.

My only complaint with the app is that sometimes things aren't very clear. If you put out a unique monster, the app will still act as though you have put out both master and minion versions of the monster. This initially created quite a bit of confusion. Also some of the directions aren't completely clear. In one mission each hero's specific gear was spread around the map. If you find someone else's gear then they immediately equip it even if they're not nearby. It seems like the intent was for each player to recover their own gear, but the app didn't say so.

Overall I'm loving the game with the Road to Legend app. The app itself is mostly great, and the game is even better as a pure co-op.

I'm not sure how meaningful my rating is here.  I feel as though I may be suffering from some Descent snobbery - as in "Pah!  It's not as good as the original!" =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.75
An improved version of the classic dungeon crawler that streamlines efficiently the experience.

Compared to the original, this one is actually playable within a respectable time frame, without losing anything in theme or flavor. There is enough variety in heroes/monsters and missions, and the replayability reaches further limits with the campaign system that quite simply elevetes the game a whole level. The game also appears to have more interesting decisions, both for the heroes and the overlord, while being quite interesting as a narrative.

There are several problems however. First of all some aspects have been overly simplyfied for the sake of speed and flow of play. Exporation has been very restricted, to the point that this can hardly be called a dungeon crawler and should be called a tactical game with a very elegant campaign system. However the game also lacks in tactics, as the positioning, movement and line of sight have been nearly nullified, making all tactics revolve around which action to pick and which enemy to target. The overlord is still there of course, and that's always a big minus for me, but at least he is much more involved now. Also there are significant balance issues with the scenarios, that are only somewhat resolved through the errata. Finally the reviving/respawning mechanism works, but is quite unfun for me as it creates plentiful situations where you don't care to attack/defeat said hero/monster.

All in all Descent 2 is a nearly enjoyable experience. It's still a fantasy luck-fest, but it's a playable and well represented one, with an involving and interesting campaign system but poor and simple scenarios. All in all, a fair choice for ameri"trash" lovers, but a pass for the rest.

Update: There are now two official co-op modes for this game, one with a certain chain of expansions that started with forgotten souls and another one with the co-op application. I find them fairly similar and both great, especially compared to the base game that is a completely different experience. I consider the Descent co-op experience among the best adventure one I've had. I currently rate the co-op part of Descent a 9.5, and the overlord one a 4, hence the middle 6.75

RATING: 7
What I like:

-It's the quintessential dungeon crawler experience. beautiful minis, great back story, fun and varied hero characters, cool dice, great gear and hero upgrades, beautiful and varied scenarios, a great big campaign and the option of playing shorter one-off games. 

What dislike: 
-The game isn't that well balanced unfortunately. This is both in regards to the heroes (Sorry dwarf but you're out), and the different maps strongly favoring heroes or overlord. Also, even if the maps are actually well-balanced (as in win rate of 50/50), often times the randomness in the game is going to make an utter humiliation of one side. Some maps actually end before the overlord even gets their first turn because of broken hero power combos in certain maps. There is also vastly different balance between 2,3 or 4 hero setup.
Too much setup. The fact that a single quest is split into two parts is cool in theory, but it means that you basically have to set up the game twice, which is a bit of a pain (with having to find the right tiles every time)
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Wow, I was really impressed with this game.  Back in the 80's I was all over Hero quest, loved it.  I was looking for a game to capture that feeling but be more up to date and this really nailed it.  

It's not that difficult to learn and the mechanics just feel really slick.  Combat is easy, one roll of the dice and you've got your range, power and surges.  Tiles are double sided, one side for inside and the other outside (which I thought was neat).  The overlord player has a goal (like the heroes) instead of just to kill the heroes.  The campaign system is really well thought out, quests are seperated into 2 parts with the first part having an effect on how easy / difficult the second part will be for either party.  Even the map at the back of the book which tells you the terrain you'll travel over in order to get to the start of the quest you're undertaking (you draw a card for different terrains which can mean good or bad things happen at the start of the quest - further you travel the more cards you draw).

Anyway, I was really impressed, great game.  Always well up for a blast on this game.  Oh and I feel like the heroes and overlord are pretty equal in terms of how much fun you'll have so i'm not gutted to have to play either (in heroquest I didn't like being the overlord player).

RATING: 8
I started my real boardgame craze this month (May, 2017) after wanting to stop playing so many video games. This is the first game I've gotten during the craze (waiting on Folklore: The Affliction and Barbarians: The Invasion) and I've played the tutorial campaign twice now with the application Road to Legend.

Man this game is awesome! It's just big. Lots of things to setup and store, especially with the mini's taking up some space. The missions can also vary wildly in time depending on how well you roll. You need a reasonably big table and I'd say roughly around 2 hours for each session. This isn't a game you rush in.

The Campaign mode from what I've seen so far (the physical book and looking on youtube) seems excellent. Easily can dump 20 hours in a campaign (which is around 9 missions I believe). 

Definitely a nice game to pick up if you like miniature combat, high fantasy, RPGs and dungeon crawling!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
A streamlined version of the first edition, the rules have been simplified where the storytelling and quests take center stage. While I found the first edition tedious, the second edition scenarios have thus far been quite short and flows extremely well. I especially enjoy how the line of sight works as well as the lack of threat tokens for the overlord. Simplifying these few things make the game significantly easier to swallow for newbies and non-hard core fantasy gamers. Plus, the pre-packaged story arc has been quite engaging and keeps our gaming group (which is Euro-centric) interested. Being overlord, the only worry I have is the repetitive nature of hunting down creatures and getting to the missions. I do hope that there will be more variation to the quests further down the scenarios to keep the gamers excited. Thumbs up for the changes.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
It's a very thematic dungeon crawler, with some strategy, and luck in rolling dices. I love that dark lord can cast spells, and he's not limited to playing with his monster army. Heroes also have many skills, including his character skills, and class skills. Some are passive, some are active, and they have one really powerful once per match skill, that can change tide of battle. Game gets better with every match, and can be very addicting.

However, it may be frustrating at some points, because understanding special rules for missions/camapaign and then making a board from the puzzle-like tiles can take a long slice of time, before we even start a game. Not to mention, a game itself can be long too. Also, your wallet might not like this game, since getting more missions, classes, characters etc. requieres you to buy more expansions. There's a lot of them, and like a base game, they can be pretty expensive for what they offer. Base game alone is okay, but sooner or later you may get bored with only few options to choose from items/classes/characters/spells/monsters, and even missions.

Anyway, it's a very good game. Would buy again, and again. With expansions.

RATING: 4
Pretty disappointing purchase for me. At first, I was excited at the concept of DnD in a box, with combat that seemed more tactical than the regular tabletop rpg. Instead what I received was a game where combat was determined largely by lucky dice rolls (which was what I disliked the most about DnD), and many scenarios which required heroes to rush to the exit over slaying monsters. For how fiddly the game was, and how long it would take me to explain the game to new players, Descent just didn't have the depth to keep me engaged. At the end I still kept this game for the minis and components, but as a game I wish there was more to combat that simple positioning and dice chucking.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A fun game for the right group of gamers, but the "one vs many" approach can lead to some animosity and argument when things get tight, especially since one of the players (the Overlord) feels like they are in a tyrannical position of authority over the other players. The game is also a little skewed in terms of difficulty depending on the number of players there are; the more players, the harder it is for the Overlord to win, which can lead to an unfair late-game (3 players feels like the sweet spot for my group).

Great flavor and mechanics here though, and the dice used for combat are intuitive and versatile.

Edit: Now that the overlord can be fully automated by the new Descent app, we've been getting a lot more use out of this game. Great fun to play with the app for those who, like me, weren't fond of the "one vs many" element.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
This game is significantly better than the first edition, which was a decent game, but way too long.  This game features more interesting quests, a streamlined treasure system, and better overlord play.  In all, it's an admirable reimagining.

That said, some of the flaws from the first edition remain.  The game still tends to go all or nothing early.  If the heroes can wipe out the early monsters and keep trucking, they will probably win the quest easily, whereas if the Overlord can put the heroes in jeapardy with an early group of monsters, she'll probably keep them on the ropes until she can pull off her own easy victory.  The combat system is still rather fiddly, and there are certain abilities that are prone to abuse.

This is now the best fast, RPG-style board game I've played, but it begs the question: Is it really that big of a deal to have an RPG-style board game?  Isn't playing an actual RPG a better experience?
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Descent has great strategic potential, a lot of replay value and the option to easily make your own content.
The game does definitely promote strategy and tactic a lot more than most other boardgames I know, so ideally it should be played with roughly equally experienced players to enjoy it fully.

RATING: 7

Once you have a dedicated group of people to play with, you can really excel into the fun parts of the story and develop real characters and strategies. It also really pushes the limit of game dynamics, pitting one person against several others. This can be taxing on friend groups, but it's important to play the game the same way you might play any other RPG -- for the fun. Even if you lose, you get some laughs in.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Fantasy Flight put their creative team to work and cleaned up the many problems with the 1st edition.  Spawning is sane, playtime is manageable, defense dice are a welcome addition (grifted from Heroscape?), the win conditions are interesting, and the scenarios are creative and well-done. Holy cow, I may actually get this played on game night and finish before the bell tolls.

I feel bad only because I sank my life savings into the original and my family's Road to Legend campaign (untouched for two years) may go unfinished.  My only consolation is that the 1st edition minis will see the light of day occasionally as "open groups" on the 2nd edition maps - thanks to the conversion kit.

The release of a clean and sober 2nd edition may also mean that Sea of Blood will remain forever unopened in my basement.  Based on the majority of reviews, though, this can only be a good thing.

[i]Update[/i]:

The many-against-overlord mechanic is finally starting to grate on me.  The biggest problem with these games is that monster actions are 100% dictated by an omniscient player, meaning that monsters always perform the perfect move/attack for any given situation.

The other problem is that many monster abilities don't matter.  The only thing that does matter is that monsters can block exits.  That's the single best power that a monster can possess, and it's the most annoying ability available - by far.  And since all monsters have this ability, by default all monsters will always be annoying.  In terms of game design, that's not a good thing.

So, introducing a degree of randomness into monster movement would have been a good idea.  Though now I'm less inclined to purchase additional expansions, so all the better for me and my pocketbook.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
I understand what the game is trying to do. It is a game that is trying to come close to a RPG in the form of a dungeon crawl.

However, this game is too much hack and slash. Except for the hack and slash dungeon crawl there is no other RPG experience except for going to town and buying things. There is a narrative story, but it makes no difference to the overall play of the game except for maybe an objective during the combat hack and slash phase which is the majority of the game. There are no puzzles or player interactions such as making critical/moral choices. As a result, I feel like there is something missing when I play. All I am really doing is moving and rolling dice. I'd prefer to play Pathfinder which presents much more of an open world and I can create my own terrain for combat.

The Descent App is a nice edition to take the role of the overlord. It also helps with the narrative of the campaign you are allegedly on. Again, to me, this addition does not change the fact that this is primarily a hack and slash system.

This is why I gave it a 6.5.

RATING: 9
Takes a good couple hours to read through all the cards so you can strategize as either a hero or DM, but once at least one person does this, the rest goes smoothly. It's easy to teach others if at least one person is the instant reference manual since, without this, the game slows to a crawl with constant rule-checking and frustrated players that didn't know what all their choices were.

Once you get your party through one or two quests though it's tons of fun to try out different classes on single quests under the advanced and expert play variants. Playing a full campaign is a big investment of time, but it's absolutely worth it. The characters are fun to level and the item shop adds an extra dose of difference in each playthrough.

With plenty of classes to switch around, this game guarantees at least  100 hours of gameplay or more before you start wanting to enter the expansions, and those multiply the gameplay even more. Plenty of bang for your buck here.

RATING: 9.3
I have never played Descent (first edition) but I really enjoy playing Descent 2.0. The balance of strategy and light role playing makes this game a ton of fun. The rules and game mechanics are easy to understand but there is depth in the strategy that greatly increases replayability. The core box includes over a dozen scenarios that can be combine into a campaign that allows players to level their characters and provide bonuses for winning the scenario. New players will definitely want to download the updated rules and errata from FFG. Several of the scenarios have game breaking flaws without the updates.

"Descent 2.0" as a whole (expansions included) is awesome. FFG is always producing new content for the game and so far each expansion has brought new mechanics and has been worth the price. The cooperative variant that was recently released was a welcome edition. The owner of Descent generally gets stuck as the Overlord. It was nice to get to play a hero.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Great components. The miniatures are really cool and I've decided to paint them to added visual appeal. Would be great to have a second set of dice though, so that both the Heroes and the Overlord can have their own set of dice. It always seems to be a search for a particular die and on which side of the table they are.

Playing as Overlord is quite fun, being able to choose your monster composition and figuring out how to defeat those pesky heroes trying to ruin your Evil day. The Overlord cards are okey, but some feel a bit lackluster.

Playing as Hero is definitely fun, and I think I enjoy that the most. Strategising with your fellow heroes and complementing each other to defeat the bad guy Overlord feels rewarding to me. I think it was a smart design choise to have Hero card decoupled from hero class, increasing the variety in the game.

A caveat for some players, it the feeling of powerlessness in certain situations. For example playing a hero that the overlord keeps focus firing on and knocking you out every turn. Or when the opposing team has such a advantageous board position that it seems like you'll be unable to achieve the objective of a scenario.

The scenario based gameplay is really the strong suit of this game. The game is not about killing each other. But fighting it often required to gain ground etc.

Campaign rules are really cool from the Hero standpoint. Being able to collect all new shiney items and new abilities is really compelling. As the Overlord of a campaign, it does feel a bit lackluster to maybe get a new card when your opposition gets all kinds of cool and nifty stuff. Not that it feels imbalanced to me, but rather less shiny and compelling. Its also quite the downtime for the overlord while the heroes bicker of which items to keep and sell or buy, and which ability they want to take next.

RATING: 7.5
This appears to be the second coming of a game that I wanted to play a lot of in my youth but I was never able to get to the table (HeroQuest). I have always been interested in this fantasy dungeon theme and spent countless hours in games like the Diablo series. While probably only playing HeroQuest once or twice as a kid, this was one of the first games that I saw when I was getting in to the hobby that I truly wanted to get. It also looked to be a step up from HeroQuest and was something that I could really get in to with a group in a campaign.

Well... I have only played it once (July 2013) and, while it was good to get that under my belt as I'm more familiar with the mechanics, it seems to be relegated to collect dust in my collection... the same way its predecessor had.

It is unfortunate because I feel that the game itself (at this point) is mechanically solid and is something that I would really enjoy. The problem is that, after being able to play it at all, it would benefit from people that like to role play a bit or get more vocal during the game (myself included). The introduction of Imperial Assault (a more favorable theme to many it seems) would appear to have put the final nail in the coffin for this game but, for some reason, I don't think that I'll be parting with it any time soon.

RATING: 9
Early thoughts:

[b]The +3 Vorpal Sword:[/b]

1. This feels like a greatest hits album - the game hits all the right notes, beats, and rhythms. Every aspect of the design is a classic of its type, yet the package is a thoroughly modern enterprise.

2. Terrific rulebook.

3. A gargantuan improvement over the 1st edition.

4. The Quest and campaign system is brilliant - why no one has implemented something quite like this before, in a *mainstream* boardgame product, is a complete mystery to me.

5. Combat resolution system is like a meld of [b]Doom[/b] and [b]Heroquest[/b]. One roll of the bones gives you hits [i]and[/i] range; the defender then rolls his dice hoping for shields - it's a good, solid system - which I like.

6. Excellent dice. Funky with particular runes for surges and whatnot, these dice always beat regular D6s in the cool contest.

7. Descent 2nd edition takes all the good stuff from [b]Mansions of Madness[/b], and then retools it for a dungeon setting, removing the fiddly search cards (which I like in that game), and a cartload of other faff. What you're left with is a lean, well-lubricated iron golem of a dungeon game. All the miniatures and tokens and stuff that'd be hidden in [b]Mansions of Madness[/b], are viewable straight from the start of the scenario here. There's zero hidden information from the delver players. The upshot of this is that the game's fast playing and snappy.

8. The experience system is really neat, introducing a deck-building aspect to the Keeper's game, amongst other elements. Meaning he can save up XP to buy traps to trick his Keeper deck out with, using the exact same deck as the campaign progresses, but now it's personalised to you, hard-nosed ex-D&D Dungeon Master. This is one of the superior fulcrums of this design. The hero players can buy equipment and skills between encounters, as you might expect - it's all so very RPG-lite, but extremely loveable. 

9. Excellent consistent artwork on everything. No cutesy poo bollocks that is found in [b]Dungeon Lords[/b].

10. I particularly like the map tiles. Each one has wilderness pictured on one side, and a torchlit dungeon setting on the flipside. FFG realise, as good DMs should, that wilderness adventures make a nice change from dungeon bashing - even if it's only really different from an aesthetic POV.


[b]The Gelatinous Cube:[/b]

1. I'm not much keen on the sculpts of the miniatures or in the choice of creatures featured. Appears that in the interests of being original and quirky, the creators have included a clutch of odd-ball monsters. I do applaud giant spiders and goblins, obviously.

2. Hate the box art displayed on the edges of the game - viewable mainly when the game's sat on a shelf in your office.

3. An extra example or two regarding the creation of monster groups and campaigns would have been perfect. I 'get' the rules, but it took 2-3 readings for this aspect to bed down in my brain.

4. No *real* magic system.

5. The flavour introduction text for the quests is pretty craptastic.


NOTE: replaced 95% of the models with painted alternatives.

RATING: 9
I've played this with the app and it was very enjoyable. Beautiful, simple to learn, nothing too complex about basic play, and the app adds some impartial randomness. It isn't a very cruel DM and that is just fine. Almost every turn is about how to best combine our team to reach the maximum outcome. Some turns will give you freedom and it's fine if one, sometimes two heroes act alone. So many expansions! So many many expansions. Could do with a bit of 3d decor sometimes instead of enemies. Enemies are varied in the actions and appearances, scenarios are just long enough, and the app does a great job of tracking things for you, guiding you along so you can feel more immersed.

RATING: 8
This is really fun.  I never played the first edition, but I've genuinely enjoyed the second edition gameplay.  Fast paced and entertaining.

I will mention that I play the overlord, and as the overlord my opinion is that you need to be able to take some enjoyment from watching the plans of the players develop.  The overlord game is much less involving and much simpler than the game run by the players.  This game is best if the players are truly cooperative and you don't have a situation where one guy is running "boss."  Our group is 2 players using 4 characters total (to experience as much of the game as possible) with me as overlord.  The two guys really cooperate together formulating plans and it's interesting to me to watch how they react to the wrenches I throw in their plans.  

In the wrong group, there could be a real hesitancy on the part of some people to take the role of the overlord, and I knock a full point off my rating for that.  If you have someone who would genuinely enjoy that role, this rating could be considered a 9.

RATING: 5
Descent second edition beats the D&D Castle Ravenloft board game, and its expatiations, in map intricacy and tiles.
It loses out in combat with its hard 1/6 miss chance on every attack role no mater what and its inability to do random dungeons. Tack on its need for a GM (the overlord) and the abysmally easy scenarios and you get a mild game that is colorful but has many other games that do what it dose but better.

RATING: 9.6
This game is so highly rated because it is EPIC. Doing a campaign in Descent can lead you down a very epic line of stories and conquests. Dungeon crawling at its finest, but while other games can pull off epicness, Descent pulls it off with mechanics that work. Combat makes sense, adding new skills and weapons makes sense. Plus, with the quest vault you can turn this game into a game SYSTEM. It could never end if you wanted it to be that way. I'll add expansions eventually, but I just finished painting all the minis for my copy. Definite 9!

RATING: 7
The gameplay is ok. The game plays smooth and quick. 

I really enjoy how they handled the skill system. It adds more tactical gameplay. They also did away with the town teleporting from 1st ed, which is good because now the action isn't interrupted by shopping sprees. 

The main issue lies with the scenario's. Some favour the Overlord and others the heroes. A lot scenario's are basically races to be the first to claim an objective and score it. The diversity in tasks and objectives for each scenario is a good thing. But it lacks the how to overcome monsters in a tactical fun and engaging way, they seem to be just roadblocks. 

Is it fun to play ? It is. Selecting skills to use, discussing strategy, finding loot and buying upgrades never get old. The scenario tells a story so it's no more kill all monsters in the dungeon now like in the first edition. 

I played through one campaign and found it very unbalanced with a lot of scenario's that favored the Overlord and even some impossible scenario's for the heroes. I like challenge but if it's too easy for the Overlord it isn't fun for anyone. Especially act 2 wasn't fun to play through. This tells me the game campaign was very badly playtested.

RATING: 9
Played first when launched a few years ago and after several plays, started to hate the imbalance of scenarios. 

Was almost deciding to sell it, when they announced the app Road to Legend, which broght me back to it, and how much the game changed since I stopped. The new expansions fixed many of the problems I had with balance, and now, with the introduction of the app I can play this game with different game modes that suits my current mood.

1 vs Many style, full coop campaign style, one shot delve style, not to mention stuff created by this community...

Pretty solid and lots of content.

RATING: 9.2
想玩DND跑团又怕花时间看三宝书？那就玩懒人版的DND“深入绝地二版”吧，二版又在其一版规则上精简细化，让这个系列的受众面更广泛。譬如，每个任务区分为二个遭遇，精简的骰子，美化过后贴近剧情的地图，领主代言人邪恶副官，以及多样化的定制扩充，把这个系列提升到了一个新的高度。当然，好评之后，玩家的眼睛也是雪亮的，FFG更会坑钱了。

【推荐度】高【冒险成长对抗，轻中度美式爱好者

RATING: 5
Oh FFG, what did you do. Yeah, Descent had some clunky rules, but in tidying them up they managed to suck all the fun out of it too. There's a few things that are better, especially the miniatures, but so many things are worse I don't even know where to begin. In fact, it's such a different game to Descent they could have released this as a companion to it rather than a replacement.
I'm sad to say I'll be returning to Road to Legend which is far superior.
